Preface
This master thesis has been prepared over five months in the period of September 2021
to February 2022 at the NEXMAP Section, Department of Physics, at the Technical
University of Denmark, DTU. It has been performed under the supervision of Kristoffer
Haldrup, Asmus O. Dohn and Martin M. Nielsen. The thesis serves as the final project,
fulfilling the requirement for the degree Master of Science in Physics and Nanotechnology
Engineering.
The work of this thesis is based on analysis of the measurements acquired at the European
Synchrotron Radiation Facility (ESRF, Grenoble) in May 2021 on the ID09 beamline in
a project led by György Vanko, at the Wigner Research Center in Physics, Budapest,
Hungary. It should be noted that I (Bianca L. Hansen) have not personally attended the
beamtime. However, I have had access to the logbook and discussed details about the
beamtime with some members of the measurement team.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge within the field of physics and chem-
istry taught at the university level. However, an introduction to the basics of time-resolved
studies and X-ray scattering is given in Chapter 2.
Bianca Laura Hansen - s163993

Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This master thesis investigates the Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering (TR-XSS)
measurements of seven derivatives of the photoswitchable transition metal complex de-
noted1 [Fe(terpy)2]2+ in an aqueous solution. Prior to this thesis, the measurements have
been performed at the European Synchrotron Radiation Facility. The objective of this
master thesis is to estimate the energy difference between the ground state and excited
quintet state by quantifying the temperature increase of the solvent, that arises from the
vibrational cooling of the excited quintet state.
The solvent temperature is quantified through analysis performed on model fitted reduced
measurements. The reduction of the measurement data includes outlier detection and
three different scaling methods. The model consists of simulated scattering signals cal-
culated from radial distribution functions and scattering signals from reference solvent
responses. This work contributes with two new methods: 1) estimation of the noise in the
measurements, and 2) identification of a proper interval used for the self normalisation
scaling. The first proposed method appears to be more robust to the choice of design pa-
rameters, compared to the traditional Polynomial method. The second proposed method
is able to identify a good Q-range candidate for self normalisation scaling.
The estimated relaxation times of the excited quintet states are in accordance with other
studies existing in the literature. Furthermore, a sequential order is observed, which is
expected from computational density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The inves-
tigations show that only the energy difference estimate for the reference measurement
[Fe(4’-H-terpy)2]2+ matches the theoretical DFT calculation.
1Iron(II)-bis-2,2’:6’,2”-terpyridine

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Photoswitchable transition metal complexes are promising candidates for molecular switch
materials, that can be used in data storage systems and display devises [1, 2, 3]. These
metal complexes exhibit bistability features. Their spin configurations of either diamag-
netic low-spin (LS) or paramagnetic high-spin (HS) can be tuned by thermal parameters
such as temperature and pressure or through light irradiation or application of a magnetic
field [4]. However, before the molecular materials are truly interesting for industrial ap-
plications, a more thorough understanding of the HS state is needed. This implies getting
a better insight to how the stability and property of the HS-state can be modified in order
to increase the lifetime at room temperature [5].
This thesis studies derivatives of the system called ”[Fe(terpy)2]2+”. The [Fe(terpy)2]2+
complex exhibits a long-lived metal-centered quintet state after photo-excitation. This
makes it an interesting model system to investigate. A transient optical absorption study
(on the same system) [6] shows, that it is possible to tune the lifetime of the excited
HS quintet state. This is done through systematic ligand substitution by varying electron
donating (ED) and electron withdrawing (EW) groups on the ligands. Since the lifetime of
the excited quintet state is mainly determined by the energy difference between the ground
state and quintet state ∆E [7], this suggests it is possible to tune ∆E. As shown in [6],
quantum chemistry computations predicts this tunability and suggests the magnitude of
the corresponding energy barrier ∆E of the different derivatives. It is diﬀicult to verify
these quantum chemistry computations with the available spectroscopy tools, since the
radiation from the quintet state to the ground state is a non radiative transition. The
transition will instead induce a temperature increase in the surrounding solvent.
The objective of this thesis is to quantify the temperature increase in the surrounding
solvent by fitting Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering (TR-XSS) data to a model.
The model consists of scattering contributions from the expected structural changes in
the solute and the changes in the solvent scattering arising from temperature and density
changes. The estimate of the temperature increase can be further used to quantify the
∆E between the ground state and excited state. The main focus of this thesis is to carry
out data reduction and analysis in order to obtain an estimate of the temperature increase
in the surrounding solvent and thereby facilitate an estimate of ∆E.
1.1
Thesis outline
This master thesis is structured as follows:
Chapter 2 introduces the background of x-ray scattering, time-resolved studies and the
transition metal complex systems of interest.
Chapter 3 describes the computational details behind the calculations of the simulated
scattering signals used in the modelling of the measurement.
Chapter 4 describes the experimental setup and gives an overview of the reduction steps
needed to perform on the data before the cleaned data can be analysed.
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
1 of 80
==PAGE:14==
1.1.
THESIS OUTLINE
Chapter 5 outlines the different reduction and analytical methods used in this thesis.
Chapter 6 describes the reduction process implemented on the data.
Chapter 7 presents the model identification, the data analysis and a discussion of the
results.
Chapter 8 provides the conclusions and future work.
The thesis applies one of the seven investigated [Fe(terpy)2]2+ derivatives as a case study.
Plots from the analysis of the other six systems are presented in the appendices together
with few other elaborations. The appendix consists of:
Appendix A Elaboration of the offset interpretation, supporting that the RDFs should
re-normalised.
Appendix B Elaboration of the flow rate and experimental time resolution. A preliminary
overview of the difference scattering signal for all systems before data reduction.
Appendix C The outlier detection influence on all the other systems.
Appendix D The chosen self normalisation intervals for all the other systems.
Appendix E The influence of the Q-axis corrections for all the other systems.
Appendix F The scaling to Liquid Unit Cell for all the other systems.
Appendix G The different model fits presented for all the other systems. The models
include Model 2C, Model 3B and Model 4. Kinetic fits of the extracted excitation fraction
and temperature at various time delays are illustrated for all the other systems.

CHAPTER 2
Background
This chapter covers some of the background theory used in this thesis. Section 2.1 provides
the theoretical background needed to understand and interpret both the results from
the Molecular Dynamic simulations (described in Chapter 3) and the results obtained
from the Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering (TR-XSS) experiments (elaborated in
Chapter 4). Section 2.2 introduces the principle behind the Pump-Probe technique, which
is the basis for the experimental technique in TR-XSS. Section 2.3 describes the scattering
components, that are used in the model of interpreting the difference scattering signal.
Section 2.4 gives insight to how the X-rays are produced at the European Synchrotron
Radiation Facility (ESRF), where the measurements were conducted. Finally, Section 2.5
introduces the derivatives of the transition metal complex [Fe(terpy)2]2+ of interest in this
thesis.
2.1
X-ray Scattering theory
X-rays are electromagnetic (EM) radiation with a wavelength in the region of Ångström
(10−10 m) corresponding to a photon energy in keV. The wavelength of the X-rays makes
the X-rays ideal for examining atomic structures of molecules since the interatomic dis-
tances of molecules are also in the order of Ångström.
The X-ray scattering contains
information about all the atoms positioned in the X-ray beam path.
The electromagnetic spectrum spans from very long wavelengths with magnitude of several
meters (denoted as radio waves) to wavelengths of sub Ångström (denoted as gamma rays).
An EM-wave can be created by accelerating charged particles and can be described as a
photon travelling with the speed of light in vacuum. The electric field of the photons
varies sinusoidally with time and is directed perpendicular to the propagation direction of
the photons. The photon beam can simply be illustrated as linearly polarized EM plane
wave. Figure 2.1 depicts the electromagnetic spectrum and the corresponding molecular
processes when photons of the specific energy are absorbed in the molecule. Thus, X-rays
belong to the high energy part of the EM-spectrum.
When an X-ray photon interacts with matter, it can either be scattered or absorbed. This
gives rise to different measurement techniques extracting different forms of information
from the investigated material. Three examples of these techniques are: X-ray Absorption
Spectroscopy (XAS), X-ray Emission Spectroscopy (XES) and X-ray Diffuse Scattering
(XDS). XAS measures the absorbed photons and contains information about the nuclear
and electronic structure. XES measures the spectrum of the emitted X-ray photons arising
from, when a core hole is repopulated after an X-ray absorption event has occurred. This
technique offers information about the charge and spin-state of the sample. XDS measures
the scattered photons created from interference between the X-ray photons and electrons
in the sample. This technique provides structural information about the sample.
The experimental method used in this thesis is called Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scat-
tering (TR-XSS) and is based on XDS. The following sections are therefore dedicated to
describe the theory behind X-ray scattering. For more thorough description of the X-ray
scattering theory, refer to [8, 9, 10].

2.1.
X-RAY SCATTERING THEORY
Rotational excitation
Ionisation
Electronic excitation
Vibrational 
excitation
Figure 2.1:
The electromagnetic spectrum for different types of radiation shown at
wavelength λ, frequency f and energy E.
In the top of the figure different molecular
processes are shown which are triggered by light absorption in the given energy ranges.
Ionisation occurs when the molecule absorbs photons in the keV corresponding to X-rays.
Electronic excitations happens when the molecule absorbs photons in the UV-VIS (eV)
energy range. Vibrational and rotational excitation can be triggered, if the molecule absorbs
photons in the infrared and THz/microwave range, respectively. This figure is modified
from [11].
2.1.1
Coherent X-ray Scattering
The scattering process of a photon on an electron can either be elastic or inelastic. In this
section the coherent elastic scattering known as Thomson Scattering will be presented.
Scattering from an Electron
The elastic scattering is characterised by the X-ray keeping the same energy, but exchang-
ing momentum with the electron. The momentum transfer is described by the scattering
vector Q = kin −kout, where kin, kout denote the incoming and outgoing wavevector of
the X-ray beam, respectively. The scattering from a single electron encountered by an
incoming linearly polarized plane wave is depicted in Figure 2.2. The electric field of the
X-ray beam makes the electron oscillate leading to radiation of spherical waves of photons.

Scattering from an Atom
An atom can be described as a collection of a nucleus of protons and neutrons, and a sphere
of Z electrons. Each of the electrons in the atom contributes with radiated spherical waves,
which provide a superposition of constructive and destructive interference.
The atom
is treated as an electronic charge distribution around the nucleus, denoted as a charge
density of ρ(r). Figure 2.3 illustrates an X-ray beam being scattered on an atom with
an electron density ρ(r). The phase differences between the radiation from the different
electrons are caused by the spatial distribution of the electrons, when the incident X-ray
beam interacts with the atom. Two non zero electron densities are marked as dark grey
spheres in Figure 2.3. When the incident plane wave hits the two marked areas, there
will be a phase difference of ϕin = kin · r between them, where r is the distance from
the origin scattered electron. Likewise, a phase difference will occur from the scattered
wave ϕout = kout · r. Taking point in the origin from the same electron, the two phase
differences will have opposite sign.
Hence, the resulting phase difference can then be
written as ϕ = (kin −kout) · r = Q · r.
The scattering length of an atom, also called the atomic form factor, can then be calculated
by integrating the phase weighted electronic scattering 1:
fatom(Q) = −r0
Z
ρ(r) exp(iQ · r) dr
(2.2)
1This is the same as a Fourier transformation of the electron density ρ(r) multiplied by the scattering
length of an electron r0.

exp(iQ · r) is the corresponding phase factor.

The Debye Equation
A molecule can be described as a collection of N atoms. Assuming that each atom n in
the molecule can be treated as an independent scatter 2 and each atom has a spherical
symmetry of electron density, the coherent scattering intensity of a molecule, denoted by
Scoh(Q), is then given by the sum of the scattering from the individual atoms:

2.1.2
Incoherent X-ray Scattering
Incoherent scattering occurs when the incoming photon exchanges energy and momentum
with the electrons of the sample.
This type of scattering is also known as Compton
scattering. The Compton scattering does not carry information about the structure of the
sample, since there is no interference between photons scattered from different electron
density volumes in the sample. However, the incoherent scattering carries information
about the momentum distribution of the electrons in the sample, which is information
about the atomic composition [8].
According to [16], the analytical expression for the Compton scattering intensity for a
molecule with N atoms (each atom denoted by n for n ∈[1, N]) is approximated by:
Sincoh(Q) =
N
X

where Zn and Scoh,n(Q) are the number of electrons and the corresponding coherent scat-
tering intensity of atom n, respectively. M, K, L are fitting parameters [16].
From Equation (2.7) the following limits can be inferred:
Sincoh(Q) →0 for Q →0
and
Sincoh(Q) →Ztot for Q →∞
(2.8)
From Equation (2.6) and Equation (2.8), it can be seen that the scattering signal for low
Q-values gives information on the structure of the sample, while the scattering signal at
high Q-values provides information about the atomic composition of the sample. Since
the atomic composition of the sample is not expected to change during a X-ray Solu-
tion Scattering experiment, the scattering signal with high Q-values is often used for the
normalisation, explained later in Section 5.4.1.
The measured scattering intensity is then the contribution from both coherent and inco-
herent scattering, as follows:
Stot(Q) = Scoh(Q) + Sincoh(Q)
(2.9)
2.2
Pump-Probe technique and energy pathways
The Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering (TR-XSS) is based on the spectroscopic
technique known as the pump-probe technique. The technique is used to measure ultra-
fast phenomena (fs to ps time scale) inside matters utilizing absorption and emission of
light. Capturing the fast phenomena requires a very fast time resolution, which can be
obtained by the pump-probe technique [17, 18]. The high time resolution of the pump-
probe technique can be exploited due to the short time duration of light pulses. Figure 2.4
(a) illustrates the principle of the pump-probe technique. The pump-pulse photo-excites
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
7 of 80
==PAGE:20==
2.2.
PUMP-PROBE TECHNIQUE AND ENERGY PATHWAYS
the sample at time t = t0, which initiates a photochemical process. After a time delay
∆t = t −t0 a probe-pulse is used to record the electronic configuration of the sample.
By changing the time delay ∆t in each measurement, multiple measurement during the
photochemical process can be gathered, providing an insight of the temporal evolution as
watching a ”molecular movie”.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.4: Figure (a) illustrates the principles of the pump-probe technique. The pump-
pulse excites the sample, and after a time delay ∆t the sample is probed by the probe-pulse,
from which scattered photons are captured by the detector. Figure (a) is from [19]. Figure
(b) presents a Perrin-Jablonski diagram showing different energy paths of the photoexcited
molecule. IC stands for Internal Conversion, which is the non radiative transition between
Si and Sj with i > j. ISC stands for Inter-System Crossing, which is a non radiative
transition between Si and Sj with i > j. VR stands for Vibrational Relaxation also known
as Vibrational Cooling (VC). Figure (b) is modified from [20].
Figure 2.4 (b) shows the different energy pathways of a photoexcited molecule.
The
molecule is excited from the ground state (GS) S0 to an excited state (ES) Sn by absorbing
energy from the pump-pulse.
From the excited state, the molecule can relax by the
different decay pathways illustrated in Figure 2.4. One pathway to emphasize is the inter
system crossing (ISC) where the electron changes spin multiplicity, which usually means
the excited system goes from an excited singlet state to an excited triplet state. The triplet
state can have a long lifetime, since it is spin forbidden to go from the triplet state to the
ground state. This is the reason for the long lifetime exhibited [Fe(terpy)2]2+, which will
be further described in Section 2.5.
Figure 2.4 (b) also shows characteristic times of the different energy pathways. The time
frame of the kinetic process that the pump-probe experiment wants to examine, is dictating
the necessary pulse duration and time spacing for the pump- and probe-pulses. In this
thesis the solvent response is investigated, which equals to a relevant time frame of some
hundred ps up to few ms. This matches the X-ray pulse duration of a synchrotron ESRF,
where the measurement has been acquired.

2.3.
COMPONENTS OF THE DIFFERENCE SCATTERING SIGNAL
2.3
Components of the difference scattering signal
The measured scattering signal from a laser excited solution can be explained by three main
interferences between the atoms in the solution: the solute-only term (denoted SSolute),
the solute-solvent cross term (denoted SCross) and the solvent only term (denoted SSolvent).
These three types of interference contributions are illustrated in Figure 2.5. The solvent-
only term is highly sensitive to the thermodynamic state of the bulk solvent arising from
changes in temperature T, density ρ and pressure P causing reorganization of the solvent
molecules. The change in the hydrodynamic parameters (T, ρ, P) occurs due to an energy
transfer from the photo excited solute to the surrounding solvent, when the solute relaxes
back to the ground state through vibrational-cooling. Since a hydrodynamic system can
be completely described by two of the three hydrodynamic parameters [21], this yields two
additional scattering contributions caused by changes in temperature S∆T and changes in
density S∆ρ.
Figure 2.5: Illustration of the interference contribution to the scattering signal from the
sample. The sample consists of the water molecules as solvent (marked as red and white
balls) and the [Fe(4’-H-terpy)2]2+ as the solute (described in more details in Section 2.5).
The pink arrow illustrates scattering caused be interference between two solute atoms, the
black arrow between to solvent atoms, and the green arrow between a solute and a solvent
atom. This figure is inspired by [22].
This study investigates the response of photoinduced molecular changes in a solute. The
signal change is very small, since the solute concentration is usually on the order of a
few mM [23]. In order to enhance the relative magnitude of the induced structure change
in the signal, the difference scattering signal ∆S is analysed instead of the direct mea-
sured absolute scattering signal S. This means, all the X-ray scattering, which are not
contributing with information to the laser induced structural changes in the sample, are
subtracted away. The difference scattering signal is calculated by: ∆S = Son −Soﬀ, where
Son and Soﬀdenote the absolute scattering, when the system is photoexcited (optical laser
on) and when the system is not being photoexcited (laser off), respectively. The difference
scattering signal components can be expressed as:
∆SSolute =

as it relaxes from an excited state initialised by the photo-excitation event.

α denotes the frac-
tion of solute in the excited state and (1 −α) denotes the fraction of solutes still in the

the difference scattering between the ES and GS multiplied with the fraction β.

and solvent, which ∆SCross describes. ∆SSolvent arises from changes in the solvent-solvent
pairs, which can be explained by three contributions. ∆Sv is the structural changes in
solvent-solvent atoms close to the solute. This only regards few molecules and is often
not regarded as an important contribution in the total solvent structural signal [24]. It
is multiplied with a fraction δ. The scattering signals ∆Su, ∆Sc, and ∆Sv are achieved
from Molecular Dynamics simulations, which are further described in Chapter 3.
The difference scattering signals caused by temperature changes ∆S∆T and density changes
S∆ρ, are illustrated in the left-hand side of Figure 2.6. These difference scattering signals
arise from the energy transfer between the solute and solvent from vibrational relaxation of
the solute. In the right hand side of Figure 2.6, the corresponding expected time delays of
the solvent responses are shown. From [21, 25], the solvent response has two characteristic
time scales:
1. Increase in temperature with constant density at time delays ∆t < 100 ps.
2. Increase in solvent density, as a response to the temperature increase at time delays
∆t in the range 10-100 ns.

∆Smodel = α ∆Su + β ∆Sc + δ ∆Sv + ∆T ∆S∆T + ∆ρ ∆S∆ρ

where α, β, δ have a relation to the excitation fraction, while ∆T and ∆ρ represent the

2.4.
SYNCHROTRON LIGHT
2.4
Synchrotron Light
As mentioned in the previous section, X-rays are emitted from electrons being exposed
to an adequate acceleration. The acceleration can be created in different ways, providing
various X-ray light sources. A synchrotron light source uses the principle, that a direction
change is an acceleration. This means that high energy electrons are circulated around
in a storage ring with a velocity close to the speed of light. Since electrons are charged
particles, the trajectory of the electron beam can be controlled by a magnetic field as the
Lorentz force dictates:
F = q E + q v × B
(2.14)
where F denotes the electromagnetic force on the charged particle q with velocity v trav-
elling around in the electric field E and magnetic field B.
Figure 2.7 shows a sketch of the European Synchrotron Radiation Facility (ESRF) syn-
chrotron. Figure 2.7 (a) and Figure 2.7 (b) show two different illustrations of the storage
ring, while Figure 2.7 (c) illustrates some of the magnets positioned in the storage ring.
The diameter of the ESRF is around 270 m amounting to a circumference of 844 m [26].
Figure 2.7 (a) and Figure 2.7 (b) indicate that the synchrotron can be described by four
main parts: the Linac, Booster synchrotron, the Storage Ring and the Beamlines.
Figure 2.7 (b) illustrates the trajectory of the electrons. The electrons are generated by
an electron gun, whereafter they in bunches are accelerated in the linear accelerator called
the ”Linac”. In the Linac the electrons gain an energy from 0 to 200 MeV before being
injected further into the Booster synchrotron. The Booster synchrotron accelerates the
electrons from 200 MeV to 6 GeV providing them enough energy to be injected into the
Storage Ring. The purpose of the booster is to supply accelerated electrons to the storage
ring, such that the synchrotron bunch current is kept as constant as possible. The booster
operates therefore only a few times a day. In the storage ring the electrons circulate with
a velocity close to the speed of light for several hours. The storage ring is formed as a
large polygon consisting of a mix of 32 straight and curved sections. The curved sections
consist of two large bending magnets marked as light blue boxes in Figure 2.7 (c). The
straight sections contain focusing magnets and insertion devices such as an undulator. The
focusing magnets keep the electrons on the ideal orbital path, and the undulator creates
an even more intense X-ray beam than the X-ray radiated from the bending magnets. As
sketched in Figure 2.7 (b), the X-rays are emitted tangentially to the orbit path in the
storage ring, where it will be directed into the beamlines positioned around the storage
ring. Each time the electrons radiate photons, they loose energy. To maintain the energy
close to 6 GeV in the storage ring, the tubes in the storage ring are kept at ultra-high
vacuum. Furthermore, radio-frequency (RF) cavities are used to restore the energy of the
electrons. The RF cavity creates an electric field, which speeds up the slow electrons and
slows down the fast electrons in the electron bunch.
Figure 2.8 (a) illustrates an undulator, which is made of a periodic array of dipole magnets
producing a temporal static and spacial alternating magnetic field in the vertical plane.
The distance between the magnets defines the undulator period λu, which is usually in the
order of a few centimeters [28]. Initially, before the electron bunch enters the undulator,
the electrons are randomly distributed within the electron bunch. The random distribu-
tion means the individual electrons radiate out of phase, which gives rise to an incoherent
radiation from the electron bunch. This is known as the ”white beam”, due to the broad
spectrum of photon energies. When the electron bunch then enters the undulator, the
alternating magnetic field makes the electron bunch oscillates in the horizontal plane Fig-
ure 2.8 (a). The undulator period λu is chosen, such that the radiation of each oscillating

Figure 2.7: Figure (a) and (b) are illustrations of ESRF storage ring, while figure (c)
gives a view of some of the elements in the storage ring. The Linear accelerator accelerates
the electron from 0-200 MeV, the Booster synchrotron boosts the energy of the electrons
from 200 MeV to 6 GeV. In the storage ring the energy of the electrons is controlled to be
at a constant level of 6 GeV. ID is an abbreviation of insertion device and BM for bending
magnet. Figures are modified from [26, 27].

Figure 2.8 (b) shows the intensity spectrum of the emitted photons after electrons have
passed through an undulator (U17) at the ID09 beamline at ESRF. The spectrum shows
how the X-ray photons are distributed with a maximum photon flux intensity at an energy
of 18 keV. The distribution is not Gaussian, since it has a tail for smaller energies. The
skewness gives this spectrum the name: the pink beam spectrum. The undulator at the
ID09 beamline is called “u17”, because the period of the magnetic field is 17 mm [29].
The data treatment and modelling are based on the assumption, that the photon energy
is monochromatic. From Figure 2.8 (b) this is obviously not completely true. However,
the distribution is still very narrow making the assumption reasonable. This is supported
by the simulations of difference spectra performed in [30], showing the influence of various
spectrum widths.

2.5.
SYSTEM OF INTEREST
2.5
System of interest
This thesis investigates different variations of Iron(II)-bis-2,2’:6’,2”-terpyridine commonly
referred to as [Fe(terpy)2]2+. These [Fe(terpy)2]2+ derivatives are developed in the group
of György Vankó. In this section, the structure of the systems will be described. This will
be followed, by a discussion of the expected influence of having different electron donating
or withdrawing groups on the ligands. Finally, the potential energy surfaces (PES) are
presented together with the expected energy pathways relevant for this thesis.
Figure 2.9 shows the ground state geometry of the [Fe(terpy)2]2+ system, which consists
of an iron atom (Fe) positioned in the center surrounded by two terpyridine ligands (also
known as terpy ligands). Each of the terpy ligands has three attachment points to the
Fe-atom, making the Fe-atom positioned in a octahedral symmetry surrounded by six
N-atoms as illustrated in Figure 2.9 left hand side. The ”R” on the ligands denotes the
electron donating (ED) or electron withdrawing (EW) substitution group. By changing
which substitution group is positioned on R, the different variations of the [Fe(terpy)2]2+
complex are created. The modified [Fe(terpy)2]2+ complexes can be written as [Fe(4’-R-
terpy)2]2+, where R denotes the substitution position on the ligands. The substitutions,
that will be investigated in this thesis, are all illustrated in the right hand side of Figure 2.9.
Throughout this thesis the different derivatives of the [Fe(4’-R-terpy)2]2+ system will be
abbreviated to FeT-R, where R ∈{ H, COOH, OMe, SMe, SO2Me, Cl and OH }, with
Me referring to CH3.
Figure 2.9: Ground state geometry of [Fe(terpy)2]2+. Left side: A schematic drawing
of the geometry. The figure is from [6]. Right side: The system in a ball-and-stick model
view, which geometry is calculated by Mátyás Pápai using density functional theory. The
atomic color code is represented to the far right. The bottom of the figure shows the seven
different substitution groups on the ligands.
In the left hand side of Figure 2.9, the Fe-N bonds are marked black and green. The
two green Fe-N bonds are called the equatorial bonds and the four black Fe-N bonds are
called the axial bonds. The theoretical study of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ [3] shows an increase in the
Fe-N bond, when going from LS to HS. Furthermore, the study shows that the equatorial
Fe-N bond has a larger increase than the axial Fe-N bond. This is the reason, why the
substitution groups are position on the equatorial axis.
Figure 2.10 shows how PES of the quintet 5MC state can be modified by changing the
substitution group on the ligands. This is predicted by computational Density Functional

Figure 2.11 (a) illustrates the DFT calculated potential energy structure of [Fe(terpy)2]2+
with arrows showing a potential energy path of the molecule. Around 300 fs after excitation
the system is expected to have arrived in the excited 5MC state also denoted 5E (5T2) in
figure (a) [3]. Based on thorough studies of the PES [3, 7, 31], a simplified pathway through

figure (b). This thesis aims to estimate the difference energy ∆E illustrated in Figure 2.11
(b) between the excited 5MC state and the GS. In the following, the excited state denotes
the 5MC quintet state.
2.6
Summary
X-rays are electromagnetic radiation with a wavelength in the order of Ångström [Å]. The
connection between the measured scattering and the structural information is obtained
from the Debye Equation. The intense X-ray beam is created by letting the synchrotron
radiation through an undulator with a certain distance between the magnets. The distance
between the magnets is chosen such that the radiation of each oscillating electron is in
phase with the following oscillation.
The difference scattering signal can be modelled as a contribution from the interatomic
solute-only, the cross term and the solvent-only interference. The solvent-only is highly
sensitive to the thermodynamic state yielding to additional contributions depending on
temperature change and density change in the solvent.

Figure 2.11:
Figure (a) shows the more complex PES together with potential energy
paths for the system. This is relevant for time scales shorter than a few ps. Figure is from
[3]. Figure (b) shows a more simplified PES relevant for this thesis. Figure (c) shows
the electronic configurations in the 3D orbitals on the Fe-center for the corresponding
molecular states illustrated in Figure (b).
Seven derivatives of the [Fe(terpy)2]2+ are investigated. The derivatives are created by
changing the substitutions positioned on the ligand. Some of the substitutions are electron
donating and some are electron withdrawing. By changing the substitution groups, it is
expected to change the energy difference between the ground state and quintet state. A
strong electron withdrawing group is expected to lead to a large energy difference gap,
while a strong electron donating group is expected to lead to a small energy difference
gap.

CHAPTER 3
Computational details
The three contributions in the difference scattering signals ∆Su(Q), ∆Sc(Q), ∆Sv(Q)
(described in Section 2.3) are calculated based on the radial distribution functions (RDFs).
Section 3.1 provides a description of RDFs in general and an examination of some of the
calculated RDFs. In Section 3.2 a brief description of the applied approach for calculating
the RDFs is outlined. A more thorough description is given in [32]. The calculation of the
difference scattering signals based on the provided RDFs is presented in Section 3.3.
3.1
Radial Distribution Functions
The Radial Distribution Function (RDF) is used to investigate the solvation structure
of a liquid around a solute. It maps the 3 dimensional structural information into a 2
dimensional space. The g(r) describes how a local density ρ(r) varies as function of the
distance r with respect to an isotropic density ρ0. Hence g(r), or more correctly gl,m(r),
is a measure expressing how likely it is to find an atom of type m in a pair distance r
away from an atom of type l [14]. A graphical representation of the determination of g(r)
is illustrated in Figure 2.5, which mathematically can be expressed as:
gl,m(r) = ρl,m(r)
ρ0,l,m
= lim
dr→0
N(r)
4 π r2 dr
1
N
V
(3.1)
where ρl,m(r) denotes the local density, and ρ0,l,m is the isotropic density given by the
average density in the total box of volume V . The local density can be calculated as the
limit value of a fraction. The fraction numerator is the number N(r) of the specific type
of atoms m found in a shell of thickness dr in a distance r away from the center atom l.
The fraction denominator is the volume of the shell 4πr2dr. Further elaboration on how
to numerically calculate the RDF can be found in [33].
Figure 3.1 illustrates a solute surrounded by solvent in a box with side length L. Depending
on whether the atoms l or m belongs to u (the solute) or v (the solvent), three different
types of RDFs can be created as shown on the right hand side of Figure 3.1. Looking at
the value limits in Equation (3.1), the RDF for a (solute, solvent) or a (solvent, solvent)
atom pair has similar behaviour:
N(r) →N for r →L meaning ρl,m(r) →ρ0,l,m, which is equal to gl,m →1
(3.2)
In case of a (solute, solute) atom pair, the RDF is:
N(r) →0 for r →L meaning ρl,m(r) →0, which is equal to gl,m →0
(3.3)
It should be noted, that the RDF for the solute-solute interference gives sharp peaks,
due to how the MD simulation has been done. The structure of the solute molecule is
restrained, which means it is very likely to find an atom at a specific distance, while it is
not likely at other distances.
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
17 of 80
==PAGE:30==
3.1.
RADIAL DISTRIBUTION FUNCTIONS
Figure 3.1: A graphical illustration of the calculation of the RDF, indicating the pair
distance r, the shell thickness dr and the box length L. Left side: Calculation of the
RDF for the center Fe-atom to an atom type denoted x, inspired by [14]. Center: The
box volume can be divided into two volumes: Vsolute with atom type u and Vsolvent with
atom type v. Right side: The three small figures show different examples of potential
RDF shapes. If atom x belongs to atom type u or v, it will look like gu,u(r) or gu,v(r),
respectively. If the RDF is calculated for two solvent atom types, it will have a shape like
gv,v(r).
3.1.1
Simulated RDF curves
Figure 3.2 and Figure 3.3 illustrate the RDFs calculated by Mátyás Pápai for the Feu-
Ov atom pair and Feu-Hv atom pair. Examining these figures, information about how the
solvent is organised around the [Fe(4’-R-terpy)2]2+ systems can be obtained. In Figure 3.2
it seems like the first solvation shell for both the LS and HS simulation can be seen with a
peak centered slightly above 5 Å and a second solvation shell with a peak value centered
a bit below 8 Å. The same features are present for the Feu-Hv atom pair in Figure 3.3.
However, the first solvation shell peak is observed at slightly higher r values and the
amplitude of the peaks is a bit less pronounced.
Comparing the RDF from the HS to the LS simulation in Figure 3.2, the red arrows
indicate that the first solvation shell has moved to smaller r values. This indicates that
the solvent oxygen atoms are packed closer to the Fe-atom in the HS state than the LS
state. However, the second solvation shell looks like the peak has moved towards larger r
values for the HS state compared to the LS state. This means the water molecules in the
second shell have been pushed a bit further away from the Fe-atom in HS state than LS
state. The same observations are also applicable for the Figure 3.3.
These trends, with the first solvation shell coming closer to the iron atom, while the
second solvation shell is moving further away in the HS state compared to the LS state,
can be explained by a lengthening of the Fe-N bond. The increase of the Fe-N bond is
in accordance with theory [3], which has been described in Section 2.5. This implies that
water molecules between ligands can come slightly closer to the Fe-atom. This is also
supported by the fact, that the amplitude of the first solvation shell is lower in HS state
compared to LS state, indicating fewer oxygen atoms are coming close. However, longer
Fe-N bond means the solute expands, pushing the rest of the water molecules longer away.
The same type of trends are also observed to similar transition metal complexes [34].
18 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:31==
3.1.
RADIAL DISTRIBUTION FUNCTIONS
Figure 3.2: RDFs, g(r), of the solute Fe-atom and the solvent O-atom in the LS and HS
simulation, respectively. The different coloured graphs indicate the different derivatives of
[Fe(4’-R-terpy)2]2+ with the substitution group R. In the right hand plot, the red and grey
arrows indicate the relative movement of the RDF peaks in the HS state compared to the
LS state.
Figure 3.3: RDFs, g(r), of the solute Fe-atom and the solvent H-atom in the LS and HS
simulation, respectively. The different coloured graphs indicate the different derivatives of
[Fe(4’-R-terpy)2]2+ with the substitution group R. In the right hand plot, the red and grey
arrows indicate the relative movement of the RDF peaks in the HS state compared to the
LS state.
It is observed that the first solvation shell is positioned at lower r for the Feu-Ov atom
pair compared to the Feu-Hv atom pair. This indicates that the water molecule is oriented
with the oxygen closest to the Fe-atom. This orientation, also seems in fine accordance,
that the water molecules are able to pack a bit closer to the Fe-atom, if the Fe-N bond is
elongated. If the Fe-N bond is elongated, it means the ligands are positioned in a distance
further away from the Fe-atom, giving the water molecule a bit more space.
Comparing the RDFs across the systems denoted by the substitution group R, the first
solvation shell shows a tendency that the electron withdrawing groups have the highest
amplitude in both Figure 3.2 and Figure 3.3. Focusing on Figure 3.2, this means that for
the ligands with electron withdrawing groups it is more likely to find a solvent oxygen in
the first solvation shell distance to the Fe-atom. This seems reasonable, when assuming the
electron withdrawing groups on the ligands will make the Fe-atom appear more positive.
Since the water molecules are polarized, the negative charged oxygen is more likely to be
close to the Fe-atom, when the Fe-atom is relatively more positive. In the second solvation
shell, the opposite trend is observable, if disregarding the FeT-H system. This means
the electron donating groups have the highest amplitude. This seems again reasonable,
when assuming, the solvent atoms in the second shell are more dominated by the ligands.
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
19 of 80
==PAGE:32==
3.2.
COMPUTATIONAL DETAILS FOR CREATING RDFS
Focusing on Figure 3.2, the negative charged oxygen molecules are more likely to be in the
second solvation shell distance, when the ligands are electron donating. This is based on
the assumption, that an electron donating ligand is relatively more positive charged than
an electron withdrawing ligand.
During the analysis of the RDFs, it turned out that the RDFs have offsets, when going
towards large radius values. The offsets in the RDFs between solute-solvent are bigger
than the offsets between solvent-solvent. This analysis is elaborated in Appendix A.1. In
order to secure the requirement from Equation (3.2) is fulfilled, a renormalisation of all
the RDFs has been performed. The renormalised RDFs are calculated as:
grenormalised(r) = gsimulated(r)
gavg
(3.4)
where gavg is the average of the simulated RDF, gsimulated(r), in the interval r ∈[18; 25] Å.
The chosen interval applied for the averaging is based on visual inspection, looking after
a flat range as large as possible at large r values.
3.2
Computational details for creating RDFs
This section describes shortly the approach of the simulation performed by Mátyás Pápai
to calculate the RDFs.
The LS and HS geometries of the solute are optimised with Density Functional Theory
(DFT) using the BP86 functional1 with the software Gaussian09 [35]. The Lennard Jones
parameters are based on the OPLS force field [36] and the TIP4P water model [37]. The
CHELPG method has been used to calculate the partial charges of the complexes. The
solvation shell structure is thermally averaged using MD, restraining the solute LS and HS
geometries to the DFT results, using hookean positional restraints. The MD simulations
done on the seven derivatives of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ have been performed with the software
Desmond [38]. The restrained structure in one of the given configurations (LS or HS) is
solvated in a cubic box of water. As mentioned in Section 2.3, the solvent-only is highly
sensitive to temperature and density changes. In order to simulate constant density, the
MD simulation of the LS and HS state has been performed with different volumes but
fixed number of molecules. The number of water molecules and the box volume for the
given [Fe(terpy)2]2+ variation and state are listed in Table 3.1 for the LS and HS state.
Two Cl−1-ions are positioned away from the restrained solute. They act as counter ions
to ensure charge neutrality of the box. The simulations have been performed with a fixed
temperature of T = 300 K for a 136 ps equilibration run, and then a 2 ns production run
using an NVT ensemble. The NVT means that the simulation is performed with constant
number of atoms (N), constant volume (V) and constant temperature (T) [39]. From the
MD trajectories the RDF can be calculated. The calculated RDFs are sampled with 0.01
Å resolution.
1In the mentioned article [32], the B3LYP* functional is used instead.

3.3
Scattering signal calculated from RDFs
The scattering signal is calculated based on the simulated RDFs using the following equa-
tion from [14]:
S(Q) =
X
l
Nl fl(Q)2 +
X
l,m
fl(Q) fm(Q) Nl (Nm −δl,m)
V
4π
×
Z R
0
r2 [gl,m(r) −g0,l,m] sin(Q r)
Q r
w(r) dr
(3.5)
where i ∈l, m is a sub-script denoting, which type of atom is used. Ni denotes the number
of atoms of type i. fi(Q) denotes the atomic form factor of atom i. δl,m is the Kronecker
delta for which δl,m = 1 when l = m, and δl,m = 0 when l ̸= m. V is the volume of the
simulated box indicated in Figure 3.1. gl,m(r) is the RDF for the atom pair l, m with a
pair distance r. g0,l,m is the RDF at infinity pair distance, which is usually set to 1 for
solute-solvent and solvent-solvent RDFs as seen in Equation (3.2). R is the half distance of
the simulation box length L. Finally, w(r) is a damping function. The damping function is
used to minimize truncation errors arising from the integration of the finite size simulation
box. Due to numerical noise, the (gl,m(r) −g0,l,m) is different from zero at large R. This
can of course also be the case, if the simulation box has been too small.
The damping function, used in this thesis, is the Zederkof damping function [24], which is
defined as:
w(r) =

(3.6)
where r < rcut denotes the distance without damping and r > rmax is the distance, where
the weight is set to zero. When using the Zederkof damping function, it is really important,
that the RDFs goes towards zero, meaning there is no offset. If there is an offset in the
RDFs, then the damping function will force the (gl,m(r) −g0,l,m) to zero, which otherwise
would have been equal to the offset. The damping from offset to zero will be observed
as a change in RDFs, which corresponds to structural change. This structural change is
therefore an introduced artifact in the signal caused by the offset. This is the reasoning
behind the renormalisation of the RDFs.

Figure 3.4 shows the influence of using the chosen Zederkof damping function with the

not going towards the limit value 1. The damped ∆S curves show to be more smooth
and without extreme amplitude at Q = 0 Å−1. The damping has not been performed on
the solute-only, since the scattering from the solute only can be calculated directly by the
Debye Equation (Equation (2.5)). This is based on the assumption that the simulation
box is large enough for the scattering from the solute-only to be treated as scattering from
a gas phase.

3.4.
SUMMARY
Figure 3.6 shows the difference scattering for the cross-term contribution ∆Sc. It can be
seen that the ∆Sc has a prominent positive peak at Q around 0.5 Å−1. Figure 3.7 shows
the difference scattering for the solvent-term contribution ∆Sv. It can be seen that the
∆Sv has an ordered peak around 0.8 Å−1 and has a broad feature around 2 Å−1 to 3 Å−1.
This is also the area where the liquid peak is expected.
Note that the unit of the simulation plots has been set to e.u./LUC. A liquid unit cell
(LUC) will be further introduced in Section 5.4.3. However, it can be considered as the
smallest stoichiometrical representative unit per liquid unit cell giving rise to the scattering
pattern. Since the simulation box consists of one solute and a number of water molecules,
it corresponds to one liquid unit cell. The concentration of the simulated solution and the
measured solution are not necessary the same. This means that it is an approximation,
when treating the simulated and the measured ∆S with same unit (e.u./LUC). However,
since it is the difference scattering signal that is inspected, only the signal response directly
caused by photo-excitation will be observed in the difference scattering signal. Hence,
the photo-excitation will only affect the molecules closest to the solute. As long as the
concentration of the solute in the simulated and measured solution is low enough, then
the approximation is valid, since the scattering from the individual solute molecules is
assumed not to interact. This means, that e.u./LUC from the simulation and e.u./LUC
from the measurements are comparable, since the extra water molecules are not visible in
the ∆S signal.
3.4
Summary
In this section the simulated difference scattering signal has been calculated and presented.
The simulated difference scattering signal is divided into three contributions - the solute-
only, the cross-term and the solvent-only. The difference -cattering signal of the cross-term
and solvent only term have been damped using the Zederkof damping function with the
parameters rcut = 10 Å and rmax = 20 Å.
Both the RDFs and the difference scattering signal show signal in approximately sequential
order with respect to the electron withdrawing and electron donating substitution groups
on the ligands.

Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 4
Experimental details
This chapter describes the experimental setup used to measure the series of seven modified
[Fe(terpy)2]2+ systems. Furthermore, the chapter outlines the data reduction process and
an overview of the measured data. The data analysed and discussed in this thesis has been
acquired at the European Synchrotron Radiation Facility (ESRF, Grenoble) in May 2021
on the ID09 beamline [40, 41] in a project led by György Vanko, Wigner Research Center in
Physics, Budapest, Hungary. The measurement team consisted of: Philipp Lenzen, Antal
Mikeházi, Kristoffer Haldrup, Zoltan Nemeth (remotely), and György Vankó (remotely).
The local contacts for the experiment were Matteo Levantino and Serhane Zerdane. Hence,
I (Bianca Hansen) have not participated in these experiments. The experimental part
of this chapter is therefore based on the logbook [42] from the experiment, as well as
discussions with some of the mentioned people involved and [20, 29, 43].
4.1
Experimental setup
The principle behind the Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering (TR-XSS) experiment is
illustrated in Figure 4.1. It is based on the pump-probe technique mentioned in Section 2.2.
The experimental setup consists essentially of an optical laser pulse (i.e. the pump), an
X-ray pulse (i.e. the probe), a liquid jet with unexcited solute molecules in solution and
a two-dimensional (2D) detector. The optical pulse excites the sample, which initiates a
photochemical process in the solute, that is described in Section 2.5. The X-ray probe
pulse is scattered by the electrons in the sample and the scattered photons are captured
in the 2D detector. By varying the time delay ∆t between the pump and probe pulse, it
is possible to probe the structural evolution of the sample over time. To emphasize the
signal change caused by the photoexcitation, the data reduction process and analysis focus
on the difference scattering images rather than the direct measured absolute scattering
images.
The sample is circulated in a loop as depicted in Figure 4.1. The solution is pumped
from a reservoir to a round capillary nozzle, where the sample is ejected as a liquid jet
with suﬀiciently high speed to ensure full sample replenishment between each pump-probe
event. The jet flows into the catcher, which returns the exposed sample back to the sample
reservoir. The reason why the sample can be circulated, is due to the small interaction
volume between the optical laser and X-ray pulse, which implies most of the returned
sample has never been exposed to the X-ray pulse. In a synchrotron TR-XSS experiment
the flow speed is usually in the order of a few m/s. As long as the flow rate is fast enough
to provide a fresh sample for each excitation pulse, the specific flow rate is not important.
However, it is important that the flow rate gives the most stable liquid jet possible, since
fluctuation of the jet has a big influence on the measurements. The flow rate is therefore
optimized for each sample. The capillaries used in the experiment have a round profile
with a diameter of 200 and 300 µm. Hence, the profile of the jet is also round. However,
it shall be noticed that, in the data reduction presented in Section 4.2, the jet is assumed
to have a liquid sheet shape. This assumption can be justified by the fact, that the X-ray
spot size is much smaller than the optical laser spot size and the thickness of the jet.
Ultrafast X-ray investigations

Figure 4.1:
Illustration of a Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering (TR-XSS) ex-
periment using a liquid sample. The sample is excited with the laser pump pulse from a
Ti:sapphire laser tuned to 560 nm, and probed with the X-ray pulse with an energy of 18
keV generated at the ID09 beamline at ESRF. There is a time delay ∆t between the two
pulses. Each X-ray pulse provides a scattering image. ∆t is varied between the laser and
the X-ray pulse, making it possible to probe different times after excitation.
The optical laser used in the experiment is generated by a Ti:sapphire laser with a fun-
damental wavelength of 800 nm. It is tuned to a wavelength of 560 nm using a TOPAS,
which is an Optical Parametric Amplifier (OPA). It has a pulse width much less than 1 ps
[44] and a spot size around (305×285) µm2. The laser pulse energy is set to 9.5 µJ/pulse.
To achieve the intensity of 9.5 µJ/pulse, a waveplate orientation of the optical laser is set
to 10 degrees.
The intense X-ray pulse is generated by the 17 mm gap undulator (U17) at the ID09
beamline at ESRF described in Section 2.4. The U17 undulator is used in the experiment
to create a quasi-monochromatic beam called the pink beam with an energy of 18 keV,
corresponding to a wavelength of 0.69 Å. In the ID09 beamline, choppers are used to
decrease the pulse rate from 5.7 MHz to 1 kHz as depicted in Figure 4.1. This enables
long time delays up to 1 ms and more time for the sample to be renewed. Before hitting the
sample, the X-ray beam travels through some optics, that are used to focus and collimate
the beam, consisting of one mirror and slits. The X-ray pulse has a pulse width of ∼100
ps [43] and a spot size of (50 × 30) µm2. It should be noted, that the X-ray beam is
focused to a smaller spot size than the optical pulse. This is in order to obtain, that the
excited sample volume seems evenly probed across the X-ray pulse cross section. In the
experiment the time delay between the optical laser pulse and X-ray pulse hitting the
sample is varied between -3 µs and 1 µs.
The experiments are performed with roughly 4000 to 5000 X-ray pulses per detector image,
depending on a capillary diameter of 200 µm or 300 µm have been used, respectively. The
time interval between two pulses is 1 ms making the integration time of the detector to
be 4 to 5 seconds.
The detector used for the experiment is Rayonix MX170-HS with
an active imaging surface 170 × 170 mm2 and 3840 x 3840 pixels [45]. The sample is
positioned approximately 5 cm away from the detector for all of the experiments of the
seven samples.
The scattering patterns captured on the 2D detector have to undergo
several data reduction processes, as described in Section 4.2 before being ready to be
analysed.
A summary of the experimental parameters is presented in Table 4.1 with more system
specific details elaborated in Table 4.2.

29 of 80
==PAGE:42==
4.2.
DATA REDUCTION PROCESS
In the following subsections, a short description of each data reduction step presented in
Figure 4.2 will be given step by step.
4.2.1
Raw images
Each raw image needs to be subjected to a background subtraction, known as the dark
image. Dark images are measurements performed both with the X-ray and the laser pulse
being turned off. The dark image subtraction is correcting for possible background noise
such as dark current, which might give rise to small variations in the different pixels. The
raw images are identified as .edf files. They are read out and corrected – one image at a
time.
Hence, the intensity of the 2D image can be written as:
I = Iraw −Idark
(4.1)
4.2.2
Mask and Corrections
Different types of detector and absorption corrections are needed on the 2D image, as
discussed in further detail below. The information presented in this subsection is based
on [20].
Polarisation correction
The polarisation correction is needed to correct the scattering intensity being depended
on the polarisation of the X-ray beam as mentioned in Section 2.1.1.
The polarisation correction can be written as:
CPolarisation = P

1 −(sin(2 θ) sin(ϕ))2
+ (1 −P)

1 −(cos(2 θ) sin(ϕ))2
(4.2)
P denotes the X-ray polarisation, 2θ is the scattering angle, and ϕ is the azimuthal angle
with respect to the horizontal plane.
Geometry correction
The geometry correction is a solid angle correction. It adjusts for the fact, that pixels at
large scattering angles cover a smaller solid angle than pixels positioned perpendicular to
the cross section of the scattered beam.
The geometry correction is given by:
CGeometry = cos(2θ)−3
(4.3)
where 2θ is the scattering angle. At larger scattering angle, a larger number of pixel are
covered per spherical angle. The signal to noise ratio is however still poor for these angles
[20]. The geometry correction heavily emphasizes the signal at large angle, which means
noise will be more dominating at large 2θ (corresponding to large Q-values).
Absorption correction
The absorption correction accounts for a fraction of the X-ray photons being absorbed.
This means the true intensity of X-ray hitting the sample is larger than the measured
intensity. The sample absorption correction is based on the assumption of the sample
having the geometry of a liquid sheet, that is perpendicular to the incoming photons and
the X-rays are only scattered once.
The sample absorption correction can be written as:
CAbsorption =
µs ls −
µs ls
cos(2 θ)
exp(µs ls) [−exp(µs ls) + exp(µs ls / cos(2 θ))]
(4.4)
The sample thickness ls = 0.03 cm and the absorption coeﬀicient of the sample is µs = 1.04
cm−1.
30 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:43==
4.2.
DATA REDUCTION PROCESS
Phosphor correction
The phosphor correction takes into account the absorption in the phosphor detection layer
in the detector. The Phosphor correction is given by:
CPhosphor =
1 −exp(−µp lp)
1 −exp(−µp lp / cos(2 θ))
(4.5)
where µp is the absorption coeﬀicient of the phosphor, µp = 360.0 cm−1, and lp is the
thickness of the detection layer corresponding to the thickness of the phosphor, lp = 0.004
cm.
The corrected intensity can then be written as:
ICorrected = (Iraw −Idark) CPolarisation CGeometry CAbsorption CPhosphor
(4.6)
Besides the mentioned corrections, usually a masking will also be performed. The masking
is used to correct potential shadow area. The main shadow sources are: the beamstop,
laser beamstop, sample holder cell or catcher. If shadows are seen close to the center of the
detector, this will be observable at small Q-values, while if shadows are seen in far distance
away from the center, this will be seen at large Q-values. This is due to the relationship
between the scattering vector Q and the scattering angle 2θ. The masking has not been
performed on the experimental data from ESRF. This can be seen in Figure 4.3, as the
signal has a weird shape at large Q-values. The weird shape might be caused by the fact,
that the square detector shape has not been taken into account, when doing the radial
azimuthal integration.
4.2.3
Azimuthal integration
After the previous correction step, the 2D scattering signal is azimuthally integrated, and
thereby transformed into a 1D scattering curve. Anisotropic scattering patterns are ex-
pected for short time delays (∆t < 100 ps) [46]. Since the measurements are performed
at much longer time delays, the scattering signal is expected to be isotropic, and no infor-
mation will thereby be lost by doing a radial integration. The azimuthal integration can
be written as S(2θ, ϕ, ∆t) →S(Q, ∆t). This is illustrated in Figure 4.3. The azimuthally
integration is an important step in the data size reduction, since the magnitude of the
data files is compressed from GB to MB.
Figure 4.3: Sketch of the azimulthal integration step in the reduction process that trans-
forms the 2D data to a 1D curve. The integration is performed by averaging the radially
isotropic scattering signal in angular bins. The fact that no mask has been used, can be
seen in the scattering for big Q-values.
4.2.4
Outlier rejection
The difference scattering signal has a magnitude of ∆S/S ≪1% [30]. The magnitude of
the scattering varies due to fluctuations in the total intensity caused by changes in the
synchrotron bunch current and instrumental drifts. These fluctuations in the intensity
or other external effect captured in the absolute scattering signal have a huge influence
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
31 of 80
==PAGE:44==
4.2.
DATA REDUCTION PROCESS
on the difference scattering signal, which even might deform the signal. Hence, outliers
must therefore to be identified and removed. The outliers can be determined based on
statistical comparison between the measurements performed at the same ∆t and same
sample system.
In this thesis outliers have been detected using the Chauvenet criterion, which is presented
in Section 6.1.
4.2.5
Self normalisation
The magnitude of the scattering signal varies across measurements performed at the same
∆t, during an experiment. Self normalisation scaling is performed to correct for variations,
which have not been that severe that the scattering signal has been removed during the
outlier detection.
The magnitude of the scattering varies due to the prior mentioned
fluctuations in the total intensity caused by changes in the synchrotron bunch current
and instrumental drifts. The magnitude of the scattering signal is also influenced by the
fluctuations in the liquid sheet thickness and solute concentration. Different quantitative
scalings are performed such as self normalisation and scaling to liquid unit cell. During
the experiment at the ESRF, the measurement team has chosen a Q-range = [4; 8] Å−1 for
the self normalisation. Section 6.3.1 presents in further details, how the self normalisation
has been performed in this thesis.
4.2.6
Off subtraction
Figure 4.4 shows an example of a structure of a time sequence scan. The ∆S curves are
created by subtracting the average of the two nearest ”laser off” reference scattering data
sets from a ”laser on” scattering data set. Within each time sequence i, the ”laser off
(reference)” is present more frequently, while the other time delays ∆t are present only
once. j counts the number of ”laser off (reference)” in each time scan. The reason for
subtracting with an average of closest laser off references is to minimise the effects of long
term drifts in the intensity of the incoming X-rays. The difference scattering signal is
calculated as:
∆Smeas = Slaser on −1
2 (Slaser oﬀ,before + Slaser oﬀ,after)
(4.7)
Time sequence

“Laser off” denotes when the optical laser arrives before the X-ray probe. “Laser on” denotes the opposite.
Figure 4.4: Example of a time sequence structure of a scan. Within the time sequence
scan an alternating sequence of ”Laser on” and ”Laser off” is present. The ”Laser off”
reference is occurring more frequent than the others, in order to be able to minimize
the effects of drift by averaging the nearest ”Laser off” reference in the ”off subtraction”
process. j counts the number of -3 µs repetitions in a time sequence i.
4.2.7
Averaging
For each ∆t multiple measurements have been carried out. An average of all files with
the same ∆t is performed in order to reduce noise and stochastic fluctuations encountered
32 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:45==
4.3.
DATA OVERVIEW
in each measurement. The more measurements contributing to the average, the better
the signal to noise ratio (SNR) and signal to artifact ratio (SAR) will become. For the
seven derivatives of [Fe(terpy)2]2+, the number of repeated measurement at each ∆t varies
between 19 to 245.
4.2.8
Q-scaling
During the experiment the distance between the sample and detector is measured with a
ruler giving a measurement uncertainty in the magnitude of millimeters. This uncertainty
influences the Q-range, since the scattering angle and thereby also the scattering vector Q
are calculated from the sample-detector distance. A Q-scaling is performed to correct this
uncertainty, which is elaborated in Section 5.4.2 and shown for the data in Section 6.3.2.
4.2.9
LUC-scaling
The Liquid Unit Cell (LUC) scaling is to transform the arbitrary units to electron units
per LUC, which later on is used to quantify the physics behind the observed dynamics.
Section 6.3.3 presents the LUC-scaling performed in this thesis.
4.2.10
Analysis
Once all these prior steps have been performed, the data is ready to be analysed and fit
to a model. The analysis of the measurements is presented in Chapter 7.
4.3
Data overview
Figure 4.5 visualises two waterfall plots for the averaged ∆S signal obtained at ESRF for
the [Fe(terpy)2]2+ derivatives with substitution H. The two waterfall plots show the same
measured data. Figure 4.5 (a) depicts the data as ∆S(Q, t) as function of Q, and Figure 4.5
(b) as Q·∆S(Q, t) as function of Q. The ∆S signals are most prominent for lower Q in (a)
and for larger Q-values in (b). Similar waterfall plots for the other investigated derivatives
of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ can be seen in Appendix B.3. A general observation of all the waterfall
plots of the seven different samples, is that there is a time dependent signal for Q < 1.5
Å−1 and around 6 Å−1. This can be seen as the amplitude of the scattering varies with
time. Furthermore, it can also be observed that there is a small signal at negative ∆t
(-3 µs and -200 ps). This indicates some background contributions in form of reduction
artifacts.
4.4
Summary
In this chapter, the experimental setup for the Time Resolved X-ray Solution Scattering
method has been described. The experiment has been performed with seven samples which
all are derivatives of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ with different substitutions. The experimental details
are found in Table 4.1 and Table 4.2. Furthermore, an overview of the data reduction
work flow is presented in Figure 4.2 together with a short description of each mentioned
step in the figure. An overview of the priliminary data is visualised in Figure 4.5 for the
FeT-H system (and in Appendix B.3 for all the other systems).

CHAPTER 5
Methods
This chapter presents an overview on the different methods applied in this work. First,
the Linear Least Square method, will be presented in Section 5.1, which is a strong tool
for data fitting. In Sections 5.2 to 5.5, all the methods applied in Chapter 6 for data
reduction and treatment are presented. These methods contain: outlier detection, noise
estimation, different forms of scaling methods and Singular Value Decomposition. Two
novel methods are developed during this thesis work denoted the Highpass Filter Method
and Neighbouring Similarity Method. Section 5.3 describes the Highpass Filter Method
used for noise estimation and Section 5.4.1 presents the Neighbouring Similarity Method
used to find a good Q-range for self normalisation. Finally, Section 5.6 includes two kinetic
models used to analyse the temporal evolution of fitted parameters.
5.1
Linear Least Squares Method
All data fits in this thesis are performed using the Linear Least Squares method. The mea-
sured data are modelled by a linear combination of the different (non-linear) Q-dependent
scattering contributions mentioned in Section 2.3. A more thorough presentation of the
Linear Least Squares method can be found in e.g. [47, 48, 49].
Given N measurements (xi, yi) with i ∈[1, N], the Linear Least Squares method estimates
a function f(x) = f(x; θ1, . . . , θm) with M fitting parameters θj, j ∈[1, M], that minimizes
the residual between all the measured data yi and the model outputs f(xi), by adjusting
the fitting parameters θj. By introducing weights wi, the concept can be generalised - also
known as the General Linear Least Squares. The sum of residuals are defined as:
E =
N
X
i=1
wi [yi −f(xi; θ1 . . . θm)]2
(5.1)
This generalisation is very handy, in case the variance of the measured data points yi is
not constant. This will later be observed in Section 6.2.
The estimated values f(x) can be written as a vector y. Likewise, the linear combination of
different (non-linear) scattering contributors can be written in a matrix X with dimensions
N ×M. N is equivalent to the number of measurements, while M is the number of fitting
parameters. A diagonal weighting matrix P can be written with diagonal elements piσ2
i
with pi > 0 and i ∈[1, N]. In relation to Equation (5.1) the weight wi is equivalent to
1
piσ2
i , where σi denotes the variance of data point yi, and piσ2
i = σ2
0 with σ2
0 being the
standard deviation of the unit weight. The fitting parameters can then be estimated as
[49]:
ˆθ =

XT PX
−1 XT Py
(5.2)
To test the performance of the model fit, the χ2 value is calculated. The best fit is the
one that minimizes the χ2. According to e.g. [48, 47], the χ2 value can be determined as:
χ2 =
N
X
i=1
[yi −y(xi)]2
σ2
i
,
χ2
ν =
χ2
N −M
(5.3)
==PAGE:48==
5.2.
OUTLIER DETECTION
χ2
ν denotes the reduced χ2 with ν degree of freedom given by ν = N −M. N is the number
of data points, while M is the number of parameters in the model. A value of χ2
ν ≈1
indicates that there is a good accordance between the observed measurements and the
estimated model. χ2
ν >> 1 means that the model is not fully capturing the data, or the
variance has been underestimated. χ2
ν << 1 indicates the model is over-fitting the data,
or the variance is over estimated. If the model is over-fitting, this is a clear sign that the
model is including noise in the model fit, which is undesirable.
Thus, calculating χ2 requires knowledge about the variance of the measurement noise σ2
i .
In Section 5.3 two methods to estimate the noise σi are presented.
5.2
Outlier Detection
Outlier detection is performed on the absolute scattering signals before the difference
scattering signal is calculated. The method used to judge whether a measurement is an
outlier or not is based on the Chauvenet’s criterion. More detailed elaboration can be
found in [47, 50]. Section 6.1 describes the results of when the method is applied to the
data.
Suppose, N measurements of a specific quantity is denoted xi for i ∈[1, N] with a mean
value of ¯x and an associated standard deviation σ. According to Chauvenet’s criterion, a
measurement xi is considered an outlier and thus rejected if:
N erfc
|xi −x|
σ

< 1
2
(5.4)
where erfc is the complementary error function, given by: erfc(x) =
2
√π
R ∞
x e−t2dt. The
principle behind Chauvenet’s criterion is illustrated in Figure 5.1. Equation (5.4) implies
that if the expected outcome of finding a measurement with value xi (or further away from
the mean) is less than 1/2, then the values of xi should be discarded. In Figure 5.1 this is
illustrated with the threshold values x⋆and x⋆⋆. If xi < x⋆or xi > x⋆⋆the value xi should
be discarded. The limit value 1
2 in Equation (5.4), is also known as the magical Chauvenet
number. A greater number of measurements N, implies the quantity measurement x⋆
i is
moved further away from the mean compared to the case of a a smaller number of N.
The Chauvenet procedure is conducted twice on the data, since a measurement that
deviates significantly might shield other measurements that should also be detected as
outliers [50]. These outliers are referred as shielded outliers.

a value in the red area (xi < x⋆or xi > x⋆⋆), it is discarded, otherwise it is kept. x⋆and

Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:49==
5.2.
OUTLIER DETECTION
To be able to use the Chauvenet’s criterion, each absolute scattering signal S(Q, ∆t) has
to be transformed into a descriptive data point, such that the multiple absolute scattering
signals can be statistically compared. As depicted in Figure 4.4 the absolute scattering
signal can be grouped based on the ∆t the scattering signals have been measured at, and
ordered after the sequence of the real measurement time at which the signals are measured.
Referring to Figure 4.4, this corresponds to picking all the blocks of the same color, and
placing them in the same order the blocks are picked out. It is assumed that the S(Q, ∆t)
signals measured with the same ∆t and from the same system have been exposed to the
same experimental conditions, having therefore similar profiles. The descriptive point for
each S(Q, ∆t) signal belonging to the same ∆t and system can therefore be treated as the
fraction between the maximum point max(S(Q, ∆t) divided by the sum of the scattering
P S(Q, ∆t) over all Q-values in the measurements. This can be written as:
Sdescriptive = max(S(Q, ∆t))
P
j S(Qj, ∆t)
(5.5)
If one or multiple Sdescriptive values vary significantly from the remaining Sdescriptive val-
ues, it means that the corresponding scattering profile has a fundamental different shape
than the scattering profile of the other Sdescriptive values. If the variation of the Sdescriptive
value fulfills the Chauvenet’s criterion (Equation (5.4)), it will be marked as an out-
lier. A Sdescriptive outlier points smaller than the other Sdescriptive points implies that the
sum of the scattering values is slightly too big with regards to the maximum value, i.e.
max(S(Q, ∆t)) < PQ2
Q1 S(Q, ∆t) and vice versa. The reason for a point to become an
outlier is likely to be caused by jet-instabilities and fluctuations in the X-ray beam, which
are that significant that it cannot be corrected in the scaling. A high background signal
will make the sum of the signal bigger. The background signal is presumably dominated
by the air scattering, which will be stronger if the jet becomes thinner, and lower if the
jet becomes thicker due to the relative magnitude of the medium of the traveling path.
Outliers are treated differently whether the absolute scattering signal belongs to the off
reference scattering signal (∆t = −3 µs) or not.
If the outlier comes from absolute
scattering signal, where the ∆t are different from -3 µs, it will simply be removed from
the data. However, if the outlier belongs to the off reference scattering ( ∆t = −3 µs), the
outlier is replaced by a scattering signal that is determined in different ways, depending
on where the detected outlier scattering signal is positioned in the series. If the outlier
belongs to the first signal or the series of first signals, the outlier(s) will be replaced by
the value of the first signal, that is not marked as an outlier. The same idea applies, if
the outlier is the last signal or the succession of the last signals, then the outlier(s) will
be replaced by the value of the last good file before they are marked as outliers. If the
outlier is not belonging to the prior two cases, then these outliers will be replaced by a
Sdescriptive point calculated from the average scattering signals of the two closest signals,
which are not identified as outliers.
The reason for treating the outliers differently, when they belong to the off reference
scattering signal, is because the absolute scattering signals are used in the ”Off subtraction”
step – as described in Section 4.2.6. If off reference scattering signals are rejected, multiple
of possible good scattering signals with δt different from -3 µs also have to be thrown out
in this step. The reason the off reference scattering appear more frequently is to minimize
risk of drifts during the measurement time. Therefore, the choice to take the average of
the two closest signals not being outliers seems reasonable.
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
37 of 80
==PAGE:50==
5.3.
NOISE ESTIMATION METHODS
5.3
Noise Estimation Methods
In this section two methods to estimate the noise level of the measured data are described.
The methods are the Polynomial Method and the Highpass Filter Method. As explained
in Section 5.1 a noise estimation targeting the measured data is required for evaluation
of the model performance on the data.
The polynomial method that is described in
Section 5.3.1 is a noise estimator presented in [30] and commonly used by the research
group at NEXMAP DTU. Section 5.3.2 presents a new alternative method for estimating
the noise in the measured data. This new proposed method, denoted by Highpass Filter
Method, is developed during this master thesis work. A sensitivity investigation of the
two methods will be performed in Section 6.2.
5.3.1
Polynomial Method
The basic idea of the polynomial method is illustrated in Figure 5.2. The method estimates
the noise of the data by calculating the standard deviation of the residuals using a local
low order polynomial fit. The local model is ”dragged” across the data to estimate the
error at each Q-point. This method has two design parameters: 1) the polynomial order
n and 2) the number of intervals Nint. The number of data points Ndata in ∆S(Q) is
divided by Nint to determine an appropriate window size Nwin. The window defines the
interval, where the local fit is performed. The local polynomial of order n is defined in
Figure 5.2. The noise level is estimated as the standard deviation of the local residuals
between the measured data and fitted polynomial. The estimated standard deviation σi
of the noise with i ∈[1, Ndata] is assigned to the center Q-value of the window Q[i]
center. In
each end, when i ∈[1, Nwin/2] or i ∈[Ndata −Nwin/2, Ndata], the noise level is estimated
to the same value for Q[i]
center positioned at i = Nwin/2 or i = Ndata −Nwin/2, Ndata.
