5.3.
NOISE ESTIMATION METHODS
5.3.2
Highpass Filter Method
The idea behind the proposed Highpass Filter method is that the noise level is estimated
based on the highpass filtered measured data. The structural information in the δS signal
can be expressed as slow oscillations. This means that by highpass filtering the signal,
only the noise will be left. The filter used in this method is a Butterworth filter [51]. This
filter is known to be both robust and simple to design. It has two design parameters: 1)
the filter order k, and 2) the cutoff frequency fc. The cutoff frequency can be calculated
as:
fc =
Rc
2 dQ
(5.6)
where Rc denotes the relative cutoff value, which can take the values between [0; 1], and
dQ is the sampling resolution of Q.
Figure 5.3 left hand side shows a Fourier transformation of a randomly chosen δS signal.
From the Fourier transformed data, the δS signal is observed to have a strong signal for
low spacial frequencies, i.e. below 5 Å. This is related to the slow oscillations in the data.
The Fourier transformation indicates that the spacial cutoff frequency should be around
or above 5 Å. If the cutoff frequency is chosen too low, structural information will be
modelled into the noise estimation. If the cutoff frequency is chosen too high, not all the
noise will contribute in the noise estimation. The right hand side of Figure 5.3 shows a
Butterworth filter designed with a filter order of k = 3 and a spacial cutoff frequency of
fc = 7.76 Å. The cutoff frequency comes from a chosen relative cutoff of Rc =0.2 and
the sampling resolution dQ = 0.013 Å−1. The Butterworth filter shows that the values
below the cutoff frequency will be oppressed. The higher the filter order, the stronger the
oppression will be, and hence a smaller cutoff value can be selected.
Cutoff value
Signal information
Noise
Figure 5.3: Left side: Fourier transformation of a δS signal. A strong amplitude is
observed at small spacial frequencies – below 5 Å. A cutoff frequency is marked with a
red arrow marking the separation for where the signal is estimated to potentially contain
structural information (marked as a green region), and where it is safe to consider it as
noise (white region). The cutoff frequency is a design parameter. Right side: An example
of the Butterworth filter with filter order k = 3 and and cutoff frequency of fc = 7.8 Å.
Figure 5.4 illustrates the idea behind the Highpass Filter Method. The black dots depict
the measured δS signal, while the red dots mark the corresponding highpass filtered data.
The method estimates the noise by calculating the standard deviation of the highpass
filtered data, that are within a window of size Nwin = Ndata/Nint. Ndata and Nint are
defined in the same way as the polynomial method. The window is then dragged across
the data. Between each movement the local standard deviation σi is calculated, and it is
assigned to the center Q-value of the window illustrated as Q[i]
center, where i ∈[1, Ndata].

same value when i ∈[1, Nwin/2] or i ∈[Ndata −Nwin/2, Ndata], namely σi(Q[i]
center) with
i = Nwin/2 and i = Ndata −Nwin/2, respectively.

5.4
Various scaling methods
This section describes three scaling procedures: Self Normalisation, Q-scaling correction
and Scaling to Liquid Unit Cell. The Self Normalisation and Scaling to Liquid Unit Cell
are both performed on the absolute scattering signal, S, where the Q-scaling correction
is performed on the difference scattering signal, ∆S. The self normalisation is done to
correct for intensity variations between the X-ray pulses.
The Q-scaling correction is
performed to correct for the uncertainty of the Q-axis which originates from uncertainties
on the measured sample-detector distance. The Scaling to Liquid Unit Cell is performed
to transform the units of the scattering signal from arbitrary units (a.u.) to electron units
(e.u.) per liquid unit cell. The different scalings will be applied on the data in Section 6.3.
5.4.1
Self Normalisation
Despite the outlier detection, yet another scaling is needed on the individual absolute
scattering signal S(Q, ∆t) due to intensity fluctuation between the X-ray pulses. This
study investigates the laser-induced changes by looking at the difference scattering signal
∆S(Q, ∆t), as elaborated in Section 2.3. Since ∆S/S ≪1% [30], even small intensity
fluctuations caused by changes in the synchrotron bunch current and instrumental drifts,
have large impact in the ∆S signal. The per thousand differences between the signals
indicate the importance of the two absolute scattering signals Son and Soﬀto be in the
same intensity scale, when performing the subtraction. If this is not the case, the ∆S will
include an artifact signal, making it diﬀicult to analyse and identify the signal caused by
the excited solute.
The scaling is performed on each S(Q, ∆t) signal, where the signal is scaled by the sum-
mation of its own signal in a self normalisation interval. The normalization interval is a
specific Q-range which is applied for all signals. The self normalisation is defined by:
Snorm(Q, ∆t) =
S(Q, ∆t)
PQ2
Q1 S(Q, ∆t)
(5.7)

Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:53==

VARIOUS SCALING METHODS
where Q ∈[Q1, Q2] covers the Q-range used for normalisation, and ∆t is the time delay
between the pump and probe pulses.
The self normalisation interval should be chosen carefully in order not to loose the week
laser-induced changes in the signal, or deform the difference scattering signal. A normal-
isation interval around an isosbestic point is therefore usually chosen [21]. An isosbestic
point is constant regardless of ∆t, meaning that it is a data point that does not contain
information of structural changes in the sample. In an ideal self normalisation Q-range,
the summation of the ∆S(Q, ∆t) signal will be zero since:

A new method to find a good candidate for the normalisation interval is developed in this
thesis and will be presented in following subsection. The method is applied to the data
later in Section 6.3.1.
Neighbouring Similarity Method
Figure 5.5 illustrates the idea behind the Neighboring Similarity Method. The method is
based on the assumption, that choosing different Q-ranges around the isosbestic point for
the normalisation will yield the same result. In other words, the ∆S(Q, ∆t) signal does
not change depending on the used normalisation Q-range around the isosbestic point. As
seen in Figure 5.5, a main Q-range is chosen (marked with grey) which will be examined
for containing isosbestic areas. The main Q-range can then be divided into Nint number
of Q-ranges denoted as dQi, where i ∈[1, Nint]. Each of the smaller Q-ranges are used as
normalisation intervals of the absolute scattering signal, as follows:

5.4.2
Q-axis scaling
In this section, the analytical expression for correction of the Q-axis is presented, followed
by a description of how it is applied on the data. The need for a Q-correction is caused
by the uncertainty on the manually measured distance between the sample and detector.
The distance is measured with a ruler during the experiment, giving an uncertainty of
few mm usually corresponding to a potential deviation of few percentages [52]. However,
even a small percentual deviation from the ”true” sample-detector distance will have a
significant influence on the model fit. This can be seen in Section 6.3.2, where the method
is used on the data for different modified [Fe(terpy)2]2+ complexes.
Figure 5.6 left ahnd side illustrates the scattering triangle with 2θ being the scattering
angle, d the distance between the sample and the detector, and h the distance from the
center of the detector to the individual pixel. The center of the detector and the distance
out to the individual pixels h are assumed to be well known, and h can therefore be
considered as a constant.
The corrected and measured sample-detector distance are denoted dc and dm, respectively.
∆d denotes the difference distance between dc and dm. The relationship between them is
given by:
dc = dm + ∆d = dm (1 + p) ,
with p = ∆d
dm
(5.13)
where p is the relative change in the sample-detector distance. A wrong measurement of
sample-detector distance influences the calculated scattering angle 2θ, and thereby also
the scattering vector Q.

5.5.
SINGULAR VALUE DECOMPOSITION ANALYSIS
5.4.3
Scaling to Liquid Unit Cell
Scaling to Liquid Unit Cell (LUC) is performed to be able to compare the measurements
with the simulations, and have physical meaningful quantities of the parameters, when
fitting the model in Chapter 7. The LUC scaling transforms the unit from an arbitrary
unit (a.u.) to electron unit per liquid unit cell (e.u./LUC). The LUC is the smallest stoi-
chiometrically representative unit of the solution, that gives rise to the scattering pattern
observed. The stoichiometry can be calculated from solute and solvent concentration. The
solvent is milli-Q water, which according to [53], has density ρwater = 0.9982 g/mL at 20
◦C giving the water a concentration of cwater =
ρwater
Mwater = 55.4 mol
L , where Mwater denotes
the molar mass of the water.
The number of water molecules per solute molecule is determined by:
Nwater = cwater
csolute
(5.16)
This means a solution with 10 mM solute concentration has 1 solute per 5486 water
molecules, and the solution with 5 mM solute concentration has 1 solute per 11082 water
molecules.
The scaling to LUC is performed by comparing the measured absolute scattering signal
Smeas from the ground state (∆t < 0) to the total calculated scattering from 1 liquid
unit cell Scalc,tot. The reason to use signal from the GS is the better knowledge of the
structure. Scalc,tot consists of coherent scattering Scoh, calculated in Section 2.1.1, and
incoherent scattering Sincoh, calculated in Section 2.1.2, from all scattering contributors
in the unit cell. The total calculated scattering from one LUC is:
Scalc,tot = Ssolute,coh + Ssolute,incoh + Nwater Ssolvent,coh + Nwater Ssolvent,incoh
(5.17)
where the scattering with subscript ”solute” refers to the scattering from the solute
molecule and counter ions in the solution. The scattering with subscript ”solvent” refers
to the reference water signal from [54] having coherent and incoherent scattering from one
water molecule with units e.u. per water molecule.
5.5
Singular Value Decomposition analysis
The idea behind Singular Value Decomposition (SVD) method is to decompose a matrix
A into a product of three sub matrices: U, S and V – illustrated in Figure 5.7 and defined
by the following:
A = USVT
(5.18)
U denotes an orthonormal matrix of size M×M, S is a diagonal matrix of size M×N and
V is a unitary matrix of size N×N. A SVD of a matrix A is illustrated in Figure 5.7,
where matrices U and VT contain information on columns and rows, respectively. Since
A has Q-dependency along the columns, the column vectors Ui (belonging to U where
i ∈[1 : M]) can also be comprehended to represent the shape of the contributing signals
in A. Likewise, since A has a time dependency along the rows, the column vectors Vj
(belonging to V where j ∈[1 : N]) represent the time evolution of the contributing
signals in A. Finally, S consists of the singular values, which represent the weights of the
contributing signals in A. The output of the SVD is sorted after the singular values in S,
as illustrated with the colors in Figure 5.7.
44 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:57==
5.6.
KINETIC MODELS
Figure 5.7: Illustration of the principle behind the Singular Value Decomposition method.
Matrix A can be divided into a factorization of three matrices: U, S and V. U consists
of information about the columns, VT about the rows and S about the relative weights of
each of columns in U and V. Figure is from [52].
The SVD method is used in Section 6.5 to investigate the measurements by identifying the
strongest signal components and checking for any artifacts that may need to be removed.
A more thorough review can be found in [55, 56, 57].
5.6
Kinetic models
In this section, kinetic models to describe the the population dynamics of the 5MC quintet
state and the temperature increase of the solvent are described. These models are applied
in Chapter 7 to analyse the temporal evolution of fitted parameters obtained from the
model fit done on the TR-XSS data. Each fit is based on data having independent time
delay.
In the time frame of the measurements, the solute is expected to undergo a relaxation
from the 5MC quintet state to the ground state. The structural response of the solute is
therefore expected to be described by an exponential decaying function given by:
γ(∆t) = γ0 exp

−∆t
τ

H(∆t)
(5.19)
where γ0 denotes the initial excited state occupation fraction, τ the lifetime of the quintet
state and ∆t the time delay after the photoexcitation event. H(∆t) denotes the Heaviside
step function that ensures the estimated excitation fraction is zero for negative time delay.
The kinetic modelling of the estimated excitation fraction for different time delays, makes
it possible to estimate the relaxation time τ of the high spin 5MC state and the initial
population fraction γ0 of the excited solute. The long time scale of the experiment (several
hundreds of ps) makes it possible to neglect the influence of the instruments response
function (IRF), on the measured signals since the IRF depends on the experimental time
resolution which is less than 100 ps.
The simplest estimation of the energy difference ∆E between the ground state and the
5MC quintet state from the solvent responses can be calculated from the relation between
capacity of the solvent CV , the temperature increase ∆T occurring during the relaxation,
and the number of molecules providing this heat. This is given by:
∆E = ∆T CV
γ0
NLUC
NA
with units:
eV
K · LUC
(5.20)
where NA is the Avogadros number, CV is the specific water capacity of the solvent with
units J/(mol·K) found to 74.893 J/(mol·K) for 20 ◦C [58].

5.7
Summary
In this chapter different methods that will be applied in Chapter 6 and Chapter 7 are
introduced and described. The methods contain outlier detection, noise estimation, self
normalisation, Q-axis scaling, liquid unit cell scaling, singular value decomposition and
two kinetic models. All fits performed in this thesis are based on the Linear Least Squares
method.
Two new proposed methods developed during this master thesis have been presented. The
method denoted the Highpass Filter Method is used to estimate noise in the measurement
data. The method denoted the Neighbouring Similarity Method is used to identify a good
Q-range candidate for self normalisation scaling.

Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 6
Data Reduction and Treatment
In this chapter the methods presented in Chapter 5 for data reduction will be used on
the measurement data. The results from the outlier detection are shown in Section 6.1.
In Section 6.2 a sensitivity analysis is performed on the two noise estimation methods
presented in Section 5.3. The different scalings methods including Self Normalisation, Q-
axis Scaling and Scaling to Liquid Unit cell, are treated in Section 6.3. Section 6.4 presents
the data after the mentioned reduction and treatment. Finally, Section 6.5 presents the
singular value decomposition analysis of the reduced data.
6.1
Outlier detection
Figure 6.1 illustrates the detected outliers based on the descriptive scattering Sdescriptive
points (defined in Equation (5.5)) for system FeT-H for two time delays: ∆t = -3 µs and
∆t = 200 ps. The grey circles show the Sdescriptive points for the absolute scattering signal
in the given ∆t. The red and dark blue circles show the outliers detected the first and
second time that the Chauvenet’s criteria is applied. The outliers detected during the
second run are known as shielded outliers. The rosa and light blue circles are related
to ∆t = -3 µs. These circles show the replaced Sdescriptive points for the outliers caught
during the first and the second filtering, respectively. Notice that the −3 µs case has
more files compared to any of the other time delays. This is because the off scattering
measurement is performed more frequently in order to be able to correct for possible drift,
as mentioned in Section 4.2.6.
Figure 6.2 shows the corresponding absolute scattering signals as depicted in Figure 6.1.
The same color codes are applied as in Figure 6.1.
Similar figures to Figure 6.1 and
Figure 6.2 are plotted for all the systems and time delays in Appendix C, along with a
2D plot of S(Q) as function of file No. with and without rejection of signals marked as
outliers.
Based on a careful visual examination, a standard deviation of 1.5σ has been chosen in the
detection of the outliers using the Chauvenet’s criterion. The standard deviation of 1.5σ
appears to be the best compromise across the investigated systems. From visual inspection
of Figure 6.1 and the similar figures in Appendix C, the detected Sdescriptive points seem
reasonable. Since the number of data points between the investigated systems varies, the
significance of the chosen standard deviation of 1.5σ changes. The harshest rejection is
seen for system FeT-SO2Me, where ∼10% of the measurements are rejected at one ∆t.
According to [30], this is acceptable.
The analysis of Figure 6.1 along with all the similar figures in Appendix C reveals a
tendency indicating that the discarded points typically have a lower value. Based on the
definition of the Sdescriptive points in Equation (5.5), the trend indicates that the sum
of the signal is relatively larger than the maximum value of the signal. As discussed in
Section 5.2, this is an indication that the outliers have a higher relative background. Notice
in Figure 6.2 that the liquid peak at Q ≈2 Å−1 is lower for the outliers. Furthermore, it
looks like the scattering for Q-values below 1 Å−1 is relatively to the liquid peak higher
for the outliers when compared to non-outlier signals. Since air scattering becomes more
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
47 of 80
==PAGE:60==
6.1.
OUTLIER DETECTION
dominating for Q below 1 Å−1 and the liquid scattering peak is observed to be lower,
it looks like the outlier is caused by scattering through a thinner jet. This means, the
observed tendency of the discarded points typically have a lower value, points to the
fluctuation of the jet more frequently makes the jet thinner.
This tendency might be
explained by the dynamic and complicated nature of the jet. The jet can be influenced by
many things. One is the sample may deposit to crystal accumulating near the nozzle and
clogging the jet. This together with air bobbles will lead to flicker making the jet more
turbulent once in a while.

6.2.
NOISE ESTIMATION
6.2
Noise Estimation
In this section the sensitivities of the Polynomial Method and the proposed Highpass
Filter Method, outlined in Section 5.3, are investigated and compared. The methods are
evaluated on the measured ∆S signal, depicted in Figure 6.3. As mentioned in Section 5.3,
the Polynomial Method depends on the polynomial order n and the number of intervals
Nint, while the Highpass Filter Method depends on the filter order k, the cutoff frequency
fc and the number of intervals Nint.

6.2.1
Sensitivity of the Polynomial Method
Figure 6.4 shows the sensitivity investigation of the Polynomial Method. In the left figure,
the influence on changing Nint is investigated, when n is kept fixed. A low Nint corresponds
to a high number of data points in each window Nwin and vice versa. The figure shows
the highest estimated noise level for the smallest Nint (corresponding to the highest Nwin)
and the lowest estimated noise for the highest Nint (corresponding to the lowest Nwin).
This makes sense since it is easier to fit a polynomial to a smaller number of points Nwin.
Note that for Nint = 5 the curve is flat both in the beginning and in the end. This is a
result of how the limits are treated, as described in Section 5.3.

Noise estimation based on the Polynomial Method
Figure 6.4: Sensitivity analysis of the Polynomial Method. The two figures show the
standards deviation estimates based on Figure 6.3 with one parameter being varied and the
other fixed. Left side: Number of intervals are varied Nint = [5, 10, 20, 40] with a fixed
polynomial order of n = 3. Varying Nint corresponds to varying the number of data point
in the window Nwin, where the local fit is performed. Right side: Polynomial order is
varied n = [1, 2, 3, 4] with a fixed Nint = 20.
In Figure 6.4 right hand side, the influence of changing the polynomial order is investigated
for a fixed number of intervals. From the figure it can be observed, that the estimated
noise is highest for the lowest polynomial order, decreasing thus with the increase of the
polynomial order. The biggest change can be seen, when changing from n = 1 to n = 2.
This is as expected, since a polynomial order of n = 1 corresponds to a straight line, which
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
49 of 80
==PAGE:62==
6.2.
NOISE ESTIMATION
fits the many oscillations in the beginning much worse than polynomials with higher order.
It seems reasonable that the estimated noise level is lowered with higher polynomial order
due to more adaptation flexibility during the fitting.
Based on Figure 6.4, the Polynomial Method appears to be highly sensitive to its design
parameters. It seems to be more stable for Nint ≈20 and n ≈3. However, it should be
noted that too high Nint and n values are not desired, since this entails a too small noise
estimation.
6.2.2
Sensitivity of the Highpass Filter Method
Figure 6.5 illustrates the sensitivity investigation based on the Highpass Filter Method by
varying 3 parameters. On the left hand side, the number of intervals are varied, while
filter order and spatial cutoff are fixed. From the plot it can be seen that the amplitude
of the estimated noise is approximately the same for when varying Nint. Smaller Nint-
values yield to a smoothing of the curve compared to larger Nint-values. This makes sense,
since smaller Nint-values mean bigger data window, and less sensitivity to smaller noise
variations.
In the middle of Figure 6.5, the filter order k is varied, while Nint and fc are fixed. The
curve with k = 1 (blue) is significantly different from curves with k > 1. This indicates
that the filter order of k = 1 is too slow with the chosen cutoff frequency, when compared
to the other filter orders that virtually provide the same noise estimation.
On the right hand side of Figure 6.5, the relative frequency Rc is varied, which corresponds
to changing the cutoff frequency fc. Changing this parameter appears to have the strongest
influence on the noise estimates. This seems reasonable, since the smaller the chosen cutoff
frequency is, the more noise is taken into the filtered data. However, making the cutoff
frequency fc too low leads to the inclusion of structural signal in the filtered data. This is
by no means desirable, since the purpose of the highpass filter is to remove the structured
signal and only preserve the noise.

an appropriate cutoff frequency value, when using theHighpass Filter Method

==PAGE:63==
6.3.
VARIOUS SCALING METHODS
6.2.3
Polynomial Method versus Highpass Filter Method
By comparing the changes in amplitude in the estimated noise level of the Highpass Filter
Method with the Polynomial Method, the Highpass Filter method appears more robust
against choices of parameters. In the remaining thesis, the noise level will be estimated
by the highpass filter. The parameters will be: Nint = 20, k = 3 and fc = 7.8 Å, and the
noise is given by one standard deviation.
Regardless of the applied method, it is interesting to notice a consistent tendency of the
estimated noise from both models in Figure 6.4 and Figure 6.5. The noise level depends
similarly on the Q-region. The noise is observed to be highest for Q <4 Å−1, lowest at
4 Å−1 < Q < 6 Å−1, and then slightly increasing at Q > 6 Å−1. This tendency seems
reasonable from a visual inspection of e.g. Figure 6.3. The high noise at low Q-values
can be explained by fewer pixels to integrate, when doing the azimuthal integration and
thereby worse statistics and hence lower SNR. Again at largest Q-values there will be fewer
pixels due to the geometry of the detector being a square and the azimuthal integrating
over a circle. Furthermore, the geometry correction, mentioned in Section 4.2.2, increases
at large angle, meaning the noise is amplified [20]. Increase in the noise can be observed
around the solvent peak at 2 Å−1. The higher level of noise around 2 Å−1 is because
the corresponding absolute scattering signal has a maximum at the solvent peak. This
means the difference scattering signal corresponds to the subtraction of two large values.
Small variations in the large peak S(Q)-values have a stronger influence than variations
at smaller S(Q)-values when subtracted.
6.3
Various scaling methods
In this section different types of scaling methods presented in Section 5.4 are investigated
on the measurement data to find the most optimal scaling values to use.
6.3.1
Neighboring Similarity Method for Self Normalisation
The application of the method described in Section 5.4.1 to investigate a proper Q-range
for the self normalisation interval is presented in the following.
Figure 6.6 shows the Neighboring Similarity Method used on the system denoted FeT-H at
a ∆t = 200 ps for different number of intervals Nint in a main Q-range of [3; 8.5] Å−1. As
mentioned in Section 5.4.1, the method looks for isosbestic areas by evaluating the Root
Mean Square Error (RMSE) of the ∆∆S of smaller Q-ranges, defined by the Nint. A low
RMSE indicates high similarity between neighboring Q-regions used for normalisation of
∆S. In other words, in these regions it does not matter which smaller area is used for in
the normalisation – a feature represented by an isosbestic region. In Figure 6.6 the same
tendency is apparent for different Nint, indicating a stability of the method. In Figure 6.6,
3 possible candidate areas for isosbestics Q-ranges are seen, namely around 4 Å−1, 5
Å−1, 7.4 Å−1. To investigate whether these candidates behave as isosbestic Q-ranges, the
method is used for different ∆t. This is illustrated in Figure 6.7 (left hand side) using a
Nint of 22 corresponding to the dark red curve in Figure 6.6. In Figure 6.7 the Q-range
between 5-7 Å−1 (marked as bad area in Figure 6.6) indeed experiences a time evolution.
This indicates that the region contains structural information which therefore is a bad
candidate as an isosbestic region, as predicted in Figure 6.6. The area of the 3 candidates
all seems independent of the time evolution as expected for an isosbestic region.

Figure 6.9 shows the influence of changing the size of the normalisation range while still
having the same Q-center at 6 Å−1 for which there is a zoom. The Qnorm-ranges are
ranked in Figure 6.9 with the smallest Qnorm of 0.5 Å−1 (represented by the brown curve)
to the widest Qnorm-range of 4 Å−1 (represented by the orange reference curve). The
insert in Figure 6.9 shows that the smaller Qnorm the lower ∆S signal. This indicates
that the information in the ∆S can be normalised out, when having a too small Q-range.
Comparing the fixed Q-center of the normalisation ranges in Figure 6.9 to the analysis of
Figure 6.8, the Q-center is positioned in an area expected to be bad for a normalisation
region. Hence, it is expected that the wider the normalisation range is, the better. A wider
normalisation range will contain both areas with high structural information and with
low structural information. A low normalisation range will only contain areas with high
structural information, which is not ideal as a self normalisation Q-range. In Figure 6.9,
a tendency showing that the wider Q-range is, the lower the absolute ∆S value is. Again,
this matches the observed tendency in Figure 6.7, where the expected best Q-range for
self normalisation yields the highest difference scattering value.
Since self normalisation is not the main focus of this thesis, this subject will not be further
pursued. However, it is a possible topic for further investigations. In Appendix D plots
similar to Figure 6.6 are presented for all systems. The black line in each figure indicates
the selected Q-range used for self normalisation Q-region. The selection is based on the
best predicted Q-region from the Neighboring Similarity Method. It should be highlighted
that three systems, FeT-COOH, FeT-SO2Me and FeT-Cl are found to be very noisy and
they will therefore be normalised with the broad Q-range from 4 to 8 Å−1.

6.3.2
Q-axis scaling
As mentioned in Section 5.4.2, there is an uncertainty associated with the measured
sample-detector distance, which influences the Q-axis and thereby the final fit. To de-
termine the correct Q-axis, the measured ∆S signal is fitted with a model consisting of
the sum of the two solvent ∆S contributions, depicted in Figure 2.6 (left hand side). For
each Q-correction of the Q-axis a fit is performed, and a reduced χ2-value is calculated.
Figure 6.10 shows the reduced χ2 as a function of the Q-correction percentages from -10
% to 5 % for the system FeT-H at a ∆t =-3 µs. The best Q-correction percentage is given
by the minimum of the χ2 distribution, which in Figure 6.10 is found to be -2.9 %.
To investigate the robustness of the determined Q-correction, the fit shown in Figure 6.10
has been performed for 5 other ∆t for all seven systems as illustrated in Figure 6.11.
The five longest time delays are examined, since it is expected that the ∆S signal from
the solute has died out. The remaining ∆S signal must therefore consist of the solvent
contributions, which matches the model used to perform the fits.

Q-corrections, and some of the confidence intervals are exceeding the Q-correction range
of the figure. These two systems are both dominated by noise causing a bad polynomial
fit to the χ2 distribution.

for the total calculated scattering of one LUC. As it can be seen in the figure, S(Q)

contributions from the solvent are highly dominating as expected from the stoichiometry.
The right hand side shows the scaled measured data together with the total calculated
scattering signal used as the reference signal. The scaling factor SF is obtained from a
fit in the Q-range = [1.7;8] Å−1. The lower limit is motivated by big discrepancies at Q-
values below 1.7 Å−1. The upper limit is defined by the Q-range of the solvent reference
measurements.
Discrepancies can also be seen at Q-values between 2 to 3 Å−1.
The
deviation for Q-values below 1.7 Å−1 is ascribed to effects from background scattering such
as air scattering, which has not been measured during the experiments. The discrepancies
at higher Q-values are not to be expected.
They might be caused by the lack of Q-
correction (hence 2θ-correction from relation Equation (5.14)) in the earlier corrections,
mentioned in Section 4.2.2 before the azimuthal integration. The discrepancies might also
be caused by artifacts in the signal, that has not been suppressed by the outlier detection
and the averaging.
An overview of all the estimated scaling factors for all systems is
given in Table 6.2. Similar figures to Figure 6.12 for the other systems are presented in
Appendix F.

6.5
Singular Value Decomposition
In this section a singular value decomposition (SVD) investigation of the FeT-H system
is performed in order to get an insight and depict the strongest scattering contributors in
the signals. The principle behind SVD is explained in Section 5.5. Figure 6.14 shows the
result of the SVD performed on the reduced Q-range [0.5:8.0] Å−1 for system FeT-H for
the first four components. The reduced Q-range has been chosen in order to reduce large
noise contributions at the lowest and highest Q-values in the SVD analysis of all the seven
systems. This Q-range will therefore also later be applied in the analysis in Chapter 7.
In Figure 6.14, it can be observed, that the first two components dominate the signal. The
first component in the left hand side (the blue graph) is very similar to ∆S curves after
short time delays. Inspecting the corresponding time evolution of the first component, the
first component is observed to decrease as function of increasing time delay. This matches
with the expectation of a decay in the fraction of molecules being in the 5MC state. The
first component therefore appears to resemble changes in signal related to the relaxation
of the solute after the photo-excitation.
The second contribution looks similar to the
reference heat from the solvent response. The time evolution of this signal is increasing,
which matches the expectation of the heat response. The initial increase can most likely
be explained by the quintet lifetime, since it has the same magnitude in slope as the first
component.
It appears like no significant components can be assigned to noise or artifacts. This is
based on the assumption that a noise contribution would be visible as smaller signals in
the Ui · Si,i with no distinct time evolution in Vi · Si,i.

6.6
Summary
In this section different investigations and choices of the reduction process have been car-
ried out and presented. The measurement data have been transformed from the raw data
absolute scattering signals to reduced difference scattering signal visualised in Section 6.4.
Outliers have been removed using the Chauvenet’s pinciple with a standard deviation of
1.5σ.
The new proposed Highpass Filter Method has also been investigated and compared with
the traditional polynomial method. The sensitivity analysis of these two methods have
shown that Highpass Filter Method appears to have a more robust performance against
choices of its design parameters compared to the Polynomial Method. In the rest of this
thesis, the noise estimation of the measurements has therefore been performed with the
Highpass Filter Method using the parameters: Nint = 20, k = 3 and fc = 7.8 Å.
Self normalisation Q-ranges of 1 Å−1 size have been determined using the novel proposed
method denoted the Neighbouring Similarity Method, for systems not containing too much
noise. For the three systems FeT-COOH, FeT-SO2Me and FeT-Cl, a broader self normal-
isation Q-range has been chosen due to too much noise.
The Q-correction analysis seen in Section 6.3.2 for all seven systems at the five longest
time delays, significantly indicated that the sample-detector distance were measured too
long during the ESRF experiments. The used correction for the different systems have
been listed in Table 6.1. The SVD analysis shows two strong contributors to the signal,
looking like the structure contribution from photo-excited solute and a heat contribution.
58 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 7
Analysis, results and discussion
In this chapter the reduced TR-XSS signals will be analysed in order to achieve an estimate
of the energy difference ∆E between the ground state and quintet state. ∆E is based on
the estimated temperature change of the solvent. Section 7.1 presents the process of finding
the best model for describing the features in ∆S signal. The model is used to extract the
temperature increase in the solvent. Section 7.2 describes how the extracted parameters
are fitted by kinetic models as well as the results of the fits. Finally, Section 7.3 provides a
discussion of the models and results with suggestion for future experimental consideration
and improvement of the analysis.
7.1
Identifying the best model
In this section different models are applied on the measured TR-XSS data presented
for the FeT-H system.
The models are fitted independently at each time delay.
The
performance of the models is evaluated by examining the residuals between data and
model, and assessing the χ2-value for 200 ps and the median of the χ2-values. According
to [3], the system arrives to the quintet 5MC state after few fs and relaxes from the
quintet state in a couple of ns. At 200 ps the system is therefore expected to have fully
relaxed into the quintet.
∆S curves from this time delay are hence used as reference
scattering for this state. The model is expanded gradually to ensure the importance of each
fitting parameter, while verifying the estimated parameters have a physical meaningful
interpretation. The models are fitted by the weighted least squares fit, as presented in
Section 5.1. All estimated parameters are displayed with an error bar or error shadowed
area with 1σ level.
The modelling is based on the assumption that only one-photon absorption from the solute
takes place. This means that the heat deposited in the solvent can be directly explained
by the thermalization of the solute through vibrational cooling of the excited 5MC state.
7.1.1
Model 1: Heat
According to [21, 25], a change in difference signal due to an increase of heat is expected
after 100 ps. This difference scattering signal occurs from reorganisation of the bulk solvent
as a response to the vibrational cooling of the 5MC state. Based on the SVD analysis and
visual inspection of the data, the solvent heat contribution is expected to dominate the
signal. Hence, the first model tested, states that the measured ∆S signal can be purely
explained by the heat response of the solvent, corresponding to:
∆SModel1(Q, ∆t) = ∆T(∆t) ∆S∆T (Q)
(7.1)
Figure 7.1 shows the result of the data from FeT-H fitted by Model 1. The model shows
strong oscillating signal between 1.5-3.5 Å−1, that appears constant with time. From the
residuals, it can be seen that the solvent heat response catches a great part of the data
shape. However, it does not capture the strong time dependent signal seen in the residual
for Q below 1 Å−1. This is also indicated by the χ2-plot, which shows high χ2-values for
short time delays and smaller χ2-values for longer time delays. The high χ2-values at short

7.1.
IDENTIFYING THE BEST MODEL
time delays match the expectation, that these time delays are dominated by structural
changes in the solute from the relaxation of the 5MC state. At longer time delays, χ2-
values are lowered indicating a better fit. This better fit coincides with the expectation,
that the 5MC state is relaxed at long time delays, leading to ∆S signal resembling pure
solvent.
Figure 7.1 shows the time evolution of ∆T obtained from applying Model 1. It increases
from 0 K at ∆t < 0 to 0.15 K at 200 ps. This fast increase corresponds to relaxation of
the system into the quintet state. From 200 ps to approximately 8 ns, it further increases
to 0.16 K. Hereafter, ∆T decreases very slowly. The temperature increase is expected to
follow the quintet life lime, as the vibrational energy should be deposited in the solvent.
The following decrease for time delays after 8 ns can be explained by the hydrodynamic
lifetime, leading to a decrease in heat after time delays of 10 ns [21]. In the two bottom
figure the model fit at ∆t = 200 ps is shown together with the magnitude of the fitting
component.
As mentioned above, the residuals clearly indicate that the model can be improved, as
there is some amount of unmodelled difference scattering signal content.
7.1.2
Model 2: Heat and Structure
As shown in Section 7.1.1, Model 1 does not capture the time dependent signal below
1 Å−1. In order to improve this, the structural response due to relaxation of the solute
from the 5MC state will be included in the model. The structural response is expected to
be described by the solute-only- and cross-term described in Section 2.3. The ∆S arising
from the solute-only term describes the internal structural changes of the solute. The ∆S
arising from the cross term describes the reorganisation of the closest surrounding water
molecules around the solute. These considerations lead to further investigation of the two
following models: Model 2A and Model 2B.
Model 2A consists of the solvent heat response and the structural response based on the
solute-only term:
∆SModel2A(Q, ∆t) = ∆T(∆t) ∆S∆T (Q) + α(∆t) ∆SSolute(Q)
(7.2)
= ∆SModel1(Q, ∆t) + α(∆t) ∆SSolute(Q)
(7.3)
Model 2B models the solvent heat response together with the solute-only term and the
cross-term:
∆SModel2B(Q, ∆t) = ∆T(∆t) ∆S∆T (Q) + α(∆t) ∆SSolute(Q) + β(∆t) ∆SCross(Q)
(7.4)
= ∆SModel1(Q, ∆t) + α(∆t) ∆SSolute(Q) + β(∆t) ∆SCross(Q)
(7.5)
Figure 7.2 shows the results from Model 2A which now include a time dependence at Q
below 1 Å−1. However, inspecting the corresponding residual Model 2A does still not fully
capture the time dependent signal below 1 Å−1. This is also apparent in the reduced χ2-
plot, which has only minimally decreased. Notice that the shape of the χ2-distribution is
still exponentially decaying, leading to the same conclusion as for Model 1, that Model 2A
does not capture the data well at short time delays. The incorporation of the solute-only
is therefore not suﬀicient to describe the structural response for the time dependent ∆S
signal.
Figure 7.3 shows the ∆SModel2B, where the additionally cross-term contribution is included.
A much stronger signal can be seen for Q below 1 Å−1, which appears to capture the

7.1.
IDENTIFYING THE BEST MODEL
corresponding feature in the data much better than Model 2A. This is reflected in the
residuals and the χ2-plot. The χ2-plot has decreased to 21 at 200 ps – almost half the
magnitude compared to Model 1 in Figure 7.1. The excitation fraction for the solute-
only term and the cross term behave similar both with regard to the magnitude and the
temporal evolution. They decreases almost linearly in the semi-log plot, meaning that
they have an exponential decreasing behaviour, as it is expected for the relaxation. The
Model 2B predicts that the excitation fraction of the excited solute at 200 ps to be around
10%, which is small compared to other synchrotron studies [59, 60]. The similar behavior
of α and β makes sense, since they both denote the excitation fraction of the 5MC state
and should be correlated. To investigate whether it is necessary to have α and β as two
individual fitting parameters a new model, Model 2C, is further tested. Model 2C consists
of the solvent heat response and a sum of the two structural responses with the excitation
fraction γ:
∆SModel2C(Q, ∆t) = ∆T(∆t) ∆S∆T (Q) + γ(∆t) (∆SSolute(Q) + ∆SCross(Q))
(7.6)
= ∆SModel1(Q, ∆t) + γ(∆t) (∆SSolute(Q) + ∆SCross(Q))
(7.7)
The results of Model 2C are illustrated in Figure 7.4. It is diﬀicult to see any big differences
in the results of Model 2B and Model 2C. This is also supported when comparing the χ2-
plot and examining the estimated parameters. Since Model 2C has one parameter less than
Model 2B but with an equivalent performance, it is clearly beneficial to select Model 2C for
further application. However, the residuals indicate that there are still some unmodelled
signal. This is addressed by further improvements to the model, leading to Model 3.
In Appendix G.1 all the fits using Model 2C on the other systems are shown.
7.1.3
Model 3: Heat and Structure (solute-only, cross) and Density
According to [21], a density related response is expected to influence the measurement at
long ∆t (10-100 ns). The ∆t of the density response is limited by the time rate of mass
transportation. Including this density term, the Model 3 can thus be written as:
∆SModel3(Q, ∆t) =∆T(∆t) ∆S∆T (Q) + γ(∆t) (∆SSolute(Q) + ∆SCross(Q))
+ ∆ρ(∆t) ∆S∆ρ(Q)
(7.8)
=∆SModel2C(Q, ∆t) + ∆ρ(∆t) ∆S∆ρ(Q)
(7.9)
Two variations of Model 3 are tested: Model 3A, with no restrictions on ∆ρ, and Model
3B, where ∆ρ is set to zero for ∆t < 10 ns to satisfy the physical expectations [21].
The results of Model 3A are depicted in Figure 7.5 with the model and residual plot in
the top figures.
The χ2 plot is very similar to the one from Model 2C. Compared to
Model 2C, the χ2 of Model 3A are a bit reduced for shorter ∆t and almost halved at the
longest ∆t. From the physical perspective in [21], an improvement in the fit at ∆t after
10 ns, is expected. However, both from the residual plot and the χ2 plot, it looks like the
density parameter catches some of the signal at shorter ∆t, which is not expected from a
physical point of view. This is also visible in the parameter plot in Figure 7.5, where the
∆ρ shows positive values at ∆t < 10 ns. A positive ∆ρ means that, the model predicts the
system to contract. Comparing the estimated temperature change parameter in Model
3A to Model 2C, it can be seen that the shape of the ∆T has changed. The predicted ∆T
is lower for short ∆t in Model 3A than in Model 2C. At ∆t > 10 ns, the ∆T is greater
in Model 3A than in Model 2C. This indicates that the fit component related to the ∆ρ
parameter captures some of the signal that more likely should have been captured by the
∆T parameter for short time delays.

prior α and β in model 2A. ∆T has likewise almost the same shape prior models. The
magnitude of ∆T has dropped a bit compared to Model 1. Bottom left: Model fit for time

7.1.
IDENTIFYING THE BEST MODEL
Figure 7.6 shows the model fit for Model 3B. The χ2 is larger at short time delays in
Model 3B compared to Model 3A, since the ∆ρ parameter is not allowed to contribute to
the fit at short time delays. Comparing Model 3B to Model 2C, it is noticed that χ2 is
unchanged for low time delays as expected, since ∆ρ is set to zero. At long time delays,
the χ2 values in Model 3B are halved compared to Model 2C. ∆T increases slower for
Model 3B than Model 3A but similar to Model 2. This is expected, since it looks like
the ∆ρ takes some of ∆T explanatory force at shorter time delays. The density change is
negative at time delays longer than 10 ns. This agrees with the physical expectation, i.e.
with a delay to the solvent heating the system is expected to expand.
In Appendix G.2 all the fits of Model 3B being applied on the other systems are shown.
Examining all the plots in Appendix G.2, it can be seen that the density change is predicted
positive for systems FeT-SO2-Me, FeT-Cl, and FeT-OH. This is not expected since it would
mean that the system contracts after the system is heated, which is not physically correct.
Model 3B performance is therefore not very reliable for the mentioned systems.
However, it seems like Model 3B in general describes the data even better at longer time
delays than Model 2C, when examining χ2-values . Model 3B will therefore be selected for
further application. At short time delays, there is still some features that are not captured
by the model. This is addressed by further additions to the model, leading to model 4.
7.1.4
Model 4: Heat and Structure (all components) and Density
As mentioned Section 2.3, there is a third structural component influencing the solvent
change. This component is the solvent-only contribution. Including this component leads
to Model 4, which is described by:
∆SModel4(Q, ∆t) =∆T(∆t) ∆S∆T (Q) + γ(∆t) (∆SSolute(Q) + ∆SCross(Q))
+ ∆ρ(∆t) ∆S∆ρ(Q) + δ(∆t) ∆SSolvent(Q)
(7.10)
=∆SModel3B(Q, ∆t) + δ(∆t) ∆SSolvent(Q)
(7.11)
Figure 7.7 shows Model 4 as function of ∆t and Q with corresponding residuals. The
residuals show that the model predicts the measurements quite well. The χ2-plot illustrates
that Model 4 is better than Model 3B at describing the signals at short time delays. This
is reflected by the χ2-value, almost being halved at 200 ps in Model 4 compared to Model
3B or Model 2C. However, at long time delays, Model 4 does not seem to perform better
than Model 3B when comparing the χ2-values. Notice that the change in temperature
between 200 ps and 8 ns is much smaller than the results of the prior mentioned models.
The change in density has a negative contribution after time delay 10 ns, as physically
expected when it is free to change.
The excitation fraction predicting the occupancy
of 5MC state has approximately the same shape as prior models. The magnitude of γ is
slightly reduced, and it appears to be negative at some of the longer time delays. It should
be noticed that, a negative excitation fraction is not physical meaningful. The estimated
error on the extracted parameters appears therefore to be underestimated, since zero is
not within the 1σ confidence interval. The δ parameter has the opposite behavior than
γ, showing that they are anti-correlated. When the electronic distribution changes at the
solute (represented by the signal-only and cross), the electronic distribution in the solvent
has to respond (signal represented by the solvent-only).
In Appendix G.3 all the fits of Model 4 being applied on the other systems are shown.
Examining all the plots in Appendix G.3, it can be seen that the density change is predicted
positive for systems FeT-SO2-Me, FeT-Cl, and FeT-OH. This is not to be expected, since
it means the system contracts after the system is heated. As mentioned earlier, this is not
Ultrafast X-ray investigations

7.2.
KINETIC MODELS BASED ON ESTIMATED PARAMETERS
physical meaningful. Furthermore, the δ parameter is positive for FeT-SMe, FeT-SO2-Me,
and FeT-OH. The δ is expected to be anti-correlated with γ, and hence not expected to be
positive. Model 4 is therefore not fully reliable for the mentioned systems. Nevertheless,
for the other systems, Model 4 yields a better modelling of the difference scattering signals.
To conclude on all the different models, Model 4 appears to fit System FeT-H the best
with physical meaningful parameters. However, with regards to the other systems the
estimated parameters of Model 4 are not as physical reliable. This is also the case for
Model 3B. Hence, in the following section the kinetic fits are performed on the estimated
parameters from Model 2C, Model 3B and Model 4. This is in order to get an idea of the
trustworthiness of the last parameters to be estimated: τ, γ0, and ∆E.
7.2
Kinetic models based on estimated parameters
In this section, the two kinetic models described in Section 5.6 are used to fit the param-
eters γ and ∆T, which are based on the estimates from both Model 2C, Model 3B and
Model 4. All these three models exhibit problems with explaining the difference scattering
at short time delays. However, Model 4 seems best at catching the time dependent signal
for system FeT-H. Since none of the three chosen models shows a better explanatory force
for all seven systems, results for all three models will be used in the following analysis.
Figure 7.8 illustrates three kinetic model fits based on the estimated excitation fraction
values, γ, provided by the three models at various time delays. The model fits in Figure 7.8
are shown for system FeT-H. The γ-values are fitted to the kinetic model in Equation (5.19)
giving γ(∆t). As an outcome of the fitting process, estimated parameters of the initial
excitation fraction γ0 and the relaxation time τ are obtained. The estimated parameter
values across the seven systems will be further elaborated in connection with Figure 7.10
and Figure 7.11.
In order to define a time delay at which full relaxation from the 5MC state to the GS is
assumed, a relaxation criteria is chosen as 1% of the γ0-value, marked as red dashed line
in the plot. The 1% of the γ0-value criteria has been used for all seven systems. Hence,
the system is assumed to be relaxed, when the fitted γ(∆t)-curve crosses the red dashed
curve. The time delays after crossing this threshold value are considered relevant for the
kinetic model of the temperature.
Figure 7.9 presents the three kinetic model fits using the estimated temperature change
parameters ∆T from Model 2C, Model 3B and Model 4. It should be noted, that the
estimated γ0 and τ from the previous kinetic model fit together with the threshold time, are
used directly as parameters in the temperature change model defined in Equation (5.21).
The fit leads to ∆T(∆t) in which the fit estimated parameters of the energy difference
∆E (i.e. the energy between the excited 5MC quintet state and the ground state) and the
Toffset are obtained.
In general the three models seem to capture the same tendency in both excitation fraction
data and temperature change data. The biggest variation between the models can be
observed in the temperature change fits.

positive temperature change fit for FeT-OMe, while Model 4 gives a positive temperature
change fit for FeT-OH. These unstable fits indicate, that there might be something, which
is not captured correctly by the models. This finding is further elaborated Section 7.3.

eter values of the energy difference ∆E across the seven systems will be further elaborated
in connection with Figure 7.12.

synchrotron experiments with similar systems.

times for system FeT-H, FeT-OMe, FeT-SMe and FeT-SO2Me are compared to τmeas,
which is an estimated relaxation time based on transient optical absorption spectroscopy
(TOAS) measurements from [6] presented in Section 2.5. FeT-H and FeT-SO2Me agree
very well with the TOAS measurement, while the systems with longer relaxation times
(FeT-OMe and FeT-SMe) are not agreeing as well.

containing electron donating groups.
7.3
Discussion and suggestion to future analysis
Clearly, improvements are needed when estimating the difference energy, ∆E, in order
to be able to use the estimated ∆E as a mean to verify DFT calculations.
General,
the estimated ∆E have large uncertainties and are estimated too large compared to the
theoretical DFT calculations. There can be many sources for the discrepancies in the
current state of the ∆E estimations. In this section different considerations for possible
improvements are discussed. These considerations can be divided into experimental and
modelling oriented considerations.
7.3.1
Experimental considerations
In this subsection, the following considerations regarding the experiment are discussed:
• Too few repeated measurements at each time delay.
• The effect of the X-ray laser not being aligned to the center of the optical laser spot.
• Higher sampling density of measurement points for time delays less than 10 ns.
A way to reduce the uncertainty on the ∆E estimates, is to increase the number of re-
peated measurements at each time delay. More measurements would reduce the influence
of outliers and thereby reduce the influence of artifacts. This is supported by the three
74 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:87==
7.3.
DISCUSSION AND SUGGESTION TO FUTURE ANALYSIS
systems FeT-COOH, FeT-SO2Me, and FeT-Cl, all showing large noise and uncertainties
during the data reduction and in the estimation of the γ0 depicted in Figure 7.10. Ex-
amining the reduced data in Figure 6.13, these systems also show signs of artifacts. The
common factor of these three systems is, that they have significantly fewer measurements
at each time delay than the other systems. The reference system FeT-H with the lowest
∆E uncertainty and closest to the DFT predicted value, has almost 100 more measure-
ments at each time delay than the system with second highest number of measurements
per time delay (refer to Table 4.2). Since ∆E values are estimated from multiple fits, high
signal to artifacts are required, otherwise the model fits will carry a too high noise level,
making it impossible to determine the energy difference.
In general, the estimated γ0, shown in Figure 7.10, appears a bit small compared to other
synchrotron measurements similar to this thesis [60, 59]. Recalling Equation (5.21), ∆E
is inversely proportional to γ0, which might have an influence in explaining, why ∆E, in
general, seems to be estimated too high. To investigate this further, an understanding of
how the γ0 can be determined too low will now be discussed. When examining Figure 7.10
and Figure 7.11, no big discrepancies are observed between the different estimated param-
eters of γ0 or of τ. Furthermore the estimated τ are comparable to other studies [6, 3]. A
possible explanation for the γ0 still being underestimated, is that the X-ray spot may not
be aligned to the center of the optical laser spot. Figure 7.13 (a) illustrates two X-ray laser
spots positioned either in the center (green disc) or on the periphery (magenta disc) of an
optical laser spot. The optical laser has a Gaussian intensity, meaning it is more likely for
the solutes molecules in the center of the laser spot to be excited than molecules at the
periphery of the laser spot. Figure 7.13 (b) shows the corresponding expected excitation
curves for the two measurement sites. The two curves have the same τ, but different γ0.
This means that, even at a non ideal position of the X-ray spot, the measurements will
still catch the characteristic exponential decay of relaxation of the excited solute molecules
and provide consistent γ0 and τ estimates (independent of the models used) with small
variances. However, γ0 is expected to be related to the center position of the optical spot.

Figure 7.13: Illustration of expected influence on the estimated γ0 in case the X-ray laser
pulse is not aligned to the center of the optical laser spot. Figure (a) shows a blue circle
representing the Gaussian intensity profile of the optical laser spot. The green and pink
discs illustrate the spots from the X-ray position in the center or the periphery of the laser
spot, respectively. Figure (b) shows the excitation fraction γ as function of time delay ∆t.
The γ0 is highest at the center γ0,c and lower at the periphery γ0,p. τ is the same for the
two measurement spots since it is characteristic time for the solute.
A way to check, whether the estimated γ0 seems reasonable, is to compare them to the
estimates from other methods, such as X-ray Emission Spectroscopy (XES) measurement.
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
75 of 80
==PAGE:88==
7.3.
DISCUSSION AND SUGGESTION TO FUTURE ANALYSIS
If there are big discrepancies between the two initial excitation estimates from the two
measuring techniques, this might indicate that the X-ray spot is not aligned to the center
of the optical laser spot.
As previously mentioned, the ∆E values are inverse proportional to γ0. Since the temper-
ature measurements come from the same X-ray spot as the γ0, a too low γ0 is not expected
to have a direct influence on ∆E. However, there might be another unfortunate effect,
that is, if the X-ray spot is not positioned in the center of the optical laser spot. This
effect could be heat waves from the center of the optical laser spot, where the solutes are
more likely to be excited and therefore more heat is generated and dispersed. It is diﬀicult
to examine the data for influence of a heat wave due to the Gaussian excitation profile. A
heat wave from the surrounding solution will imply the temperature being estimated too
high, which means the ∆E is estimated too high.
The heat is expected to move around 100 µm within 50 ns to 100 ns [61], which is rather
slow compared to the estimated τ. The systems with the longest relaxation times should
be most influenced by a potential heat wave, since the energy estimation is based on
temperature in the time period of the relaxation from the 5MC state. Examining the
temperature fits for the systems with longest τ (FeT-OMe, FeT-SMe and FeT-OH), a
clear additional heat is not observable. Furthermore, examining Figure 7.12 a clear trend
of increasing ∆E with longer relaxation time is not observable. However, notice that the
potential heat wave will introduce a tendency opposite to the tendency of the predicted
DFT ∆E calculations based on the influence of the different R-groups. Another influence
of the center spot misalignment is, that the assumption of homogeneous solute excitation
is not valid. Hence, it is really important that the X-ray spot is aligned to the center of
the optical laser spot to get trustworthy ∆E estimates.
Another experimental consideration to improve the ∆E estimates, is to increase the sam-
pling density of the measurements below 10 ns and only use signal for ∆t < 10 ns. After
10 ns the density response is expected to contribute [21, 43], thus making it more diﬀi-
cult to extract the ∆E. The influence of the density response is observable for the three
systems with longest relaxation times, FeT-OMe, FeT-SMe and FeT-OH. Examining the
temperature fits from Model 2C (without density response), the temperature clearly starts
to decrease after 10 ns.
A too large energy estimation could also be caused, if the assumption of single-photon
excitation is not fulfilled. If the intensity of the optical laser is too large, direct photo-
excitation of the solvent can happen through multi-photon absorption. This would lead to
a too high measured temperature and thereby a too high estimated energy difference. The
optical laser energy used for all the systems is 2.2 eV (560 nm). Calculating the difference
energy based on the temperature increase between -200 ps to the estimated 5MC relaxed
time delay for all the systems (e.g. 8 ns for FeT-H), none of the systems are close to exceed
2.2 eV, indicating that multi-photon excitation is not an issue.
7.3.2
Model related considerations
The following considerations regarding the modelling will be discussed:
• Need for splitting information of the kinetic regimes.
• Model lacks explanatory power at low ∆t. Questioning the validity of the constrained
structures obtained from DFT calculations.
A reason, for the ∆E values are being overestimated, can be the diﬀiculties in modelling
the density contribution at ∆t > 10 ns. At ∆t > 10 ns more kinetic regimes are en-
76 of 80
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
==PAGE:89==
7.4.
SUMMARY
countered (the 5MC relaxation and density response), making it diﬀicult to separate the
scattering contributions. The scattering contribution from the density response or solvent-
only response might compensate for other contributions, making ∆T and hence ∆E bigger
than they really are. This seems to be the case for FeT-COOH, FeT-OMe and FeT-Cl
(Model 4). As mentioned before, a solution can be to only fit the temperature for data
with ∆t < 10 ns. Another solution might be to use the simple Model 2B (without density)
to model the temperature data – and then try to include the expected density response
from [21] in the temperature model fit.
The χ2 plots for all models show a lack of explanatory power for short time delays, indicat-
ing potential improvements of the structural part of the model are needed. The difference
scattering signal is based on DFT optimized geometries, calculating the most stable struc-
ture in the GS and ES. To obtain the simulated scattering signal, classical molecular
dynamics are performed on the frozen optimized structures in a solvent. However, per-
haps this assumption, that the scattering signal can be simulated based on constrained
DFT optimized structures in a non-constrained solvent cannot be justified. Figure 7.14
shows the simulated difference scattering signal, when it is based on DFT optimised struc-
tures (red curve) or the average structures (blue curve) from the SHARC excited state
dynamics simulations.
Notice the simulation is performed on another system denoted
[Fe(bpy)(CN)4]2+. The SHARC simulations [62, 63] calculate an average geometry from
a distribution of trajectories at different time points during the simulation. Figure 7.14
shows clearly, there is a big difference in how the simulated scattering signal is calculated.
This influence is however expected to be more dominant for even shorter ∆t than applied
in this thesis. At shorter ∆t more excited states will be populated.
Figure 7.14:
Comparison between simulated ∆S signal based on geometry-optimized
structures using DFT (geop. opt. (eq)) and averaged-geometry structure using SHARC
calculations (avgr. traj. (un-eq)). The plot indicates big differences depending on the
simulated ∆S signal is based on structure in equilibrium or out of equilibrium.
The
simulations are based on the [Fe(bpy)(CN)4]2+ system. Figure is from [64].
7.4
Summary
In this chapter different models have been investigated. The best model for the FeT-H
system is identified and found to be Model 4. Model 4 contains the solvent heat, the three
simulated solute-only, cross-term and solvent-only contributions, and the solvent density
responses.
The estimated relaxation times show a sequential tendency for the difference substitution
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
77 of 80
==PAGE:90==
7.4.
SUMMARY
groups R being in accordance with the predicted DFT calculations. Furthermore, the
estimated relaxation times are comparable to the estimates of other studies.
The initial excitation fraction estimates appear a bit low compared to other similar syn-
chrotron studies. A suggested explanation is that the X-ray laser spot is not aligned to
the center of the optical laser spot.
The difference energy between the ground state and excited 5MC state is in accordance
with the DFT predictions only for system FeT-H, when using Model 4 to extract the
temperatures. The other systems show difference energy estimates generally estimated too
high, associated associated with large uncertainties. The predicted sequential tendency of
the different derivatives of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ is not observable. Many potential sources can
cause these discrepancies. Examples of this can be too low signal to artifact signal caused
by too few measurements in each time delay, and aligning the X-ray spot in the center of
the optical laser spot.

CHAPTER 8
Conclusions
The overall objective of this master thesis has been to quantify the temperature increase of
the solvent for seven samples in aqueous solutions. Based on these temperature increases,
estimations of the energy difference between the ground state (GS) and excited quintet
state (ES) have been achieved.
This quantification has been done by analysing a set of Time Resolved X-ray Solution
Scattering (TR-XSS) measurements of seven derivatives of the photoswitchable tran-
sition metal complex [Fe(terpy)2]2+ in an aqueous solution.
The measurements have
been acquired at the European Synchrotron Radiation Facility.
The seven derivatives
of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ are based on systematic ligand substitution of electron donating or elec-
tron withdrawing groups on the ligands. The substitutions are used to vary the energy of
the quintet state.
An extensive data reduction has been performed before the data could be analysed. The
data reduction steps include outlier detection, noise estimation and various scaling ap-
proaches. In regards to the data reduction process, two new methods have been developed
as part of this master thesis work. The first proposed method describes a novel way to
estimate the noise in the measurement. This new method is named the Highpass Filter
Method.
Based on a sensitivity analysis, the Highpass Filter Method appears to have
a more robust performance regarding the choice of its design parameters compared to
the traditional noise estimation method, the Polynomial Method. The second proposed
method, denoted the Neighbouring Similarity Method, is developed to identify a good Q-
range candidate to be used in the self normalisation scaling. A thorough analysis has been
performed to investigate the influence on using different Q-ranges for the self normalisa-
tion. The results have shown that there is a clear influence on the difference scattering
signal depending on the used self normalisation Q-range. A tendency indicates that the
difference scattering signal based on self normalisation with good predicted Q-ranges pro-
vides a smaller absolute difference scattering signal than the self normalisation, where a
predicted bad Q-range is used.
The difference scattering signal has been analysed by performing general linear least
squares fit on the data for individual time delays. The investigated models include differ-
ent number of contributions to explain the changes both in the solute structure and the
bulk solvent. Analysis has shown that the best model for the reference system [Fe(4’-H-
terpy)2]2+ is the model, which includes both the two expected solvent responses arising
from temperature and density increase, and the three simulated difference scattering sig-
nal arising from structural changes in the solute. Estimated parameters such as excitation
fraction, temperature change and density change have been obtained for each time delay.
The time dependent excitation fraction and temperature change have been further fitted
with two kinetic fits, providing an estimation of the initial excitation fraction, the relax-
ation time of the quintet state, and the energy difference between the ground state and
quintet state.
The estimated relaxation times are comparable to the estimates presented in other studies
Ultrafast X-ray investigations
79 of 80
==PAGE:92==
8.1.
FUTURE WORK
existing in the literature. Furthermore, the relaxation times show a sequential tendency
for the different substitution groups R, which matches the predicted DFT calculations.
Comparing the initial excitation fraction to other similar synchrotron studies, the estimates
appear lower. A suggested explanation is that the X-ray laser spot has not been aligned
to the center of the optical laser spot.
For the FeT-H system, the estimated energy difference between the ground state and
quintet state is in accordance with the predicted DFT calculation. However, the energy
difference estimates for the other systems seem generally to be too large with associated big
uncertainties. Furthermore, the predicted sequential tendency of the different derivatives
of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ is not observable for the energy difference estimates. Many potential
sources can cause these discrepancies. Two examples could be too low signal to artifact
ratio caused by too few measurements in each time delay, or a misalignment between the
X-ray and the optical laser beam.
8.1
Future work
Suggestions for further investigations could involve:
• Extend the model with structural refinement. The structural refinement provides a
relation between the energy difference and the Fe-N coordinates. The Fe-N distance
dependency can be incorporated in the model by calculating the difference scattering
signal for different molecular structures. An idea could be to obtain the different
structures by performing a sequence interpolation between the GS and ES structure
files.
• Investigate whether further data reduction for removing artifacts from the presented
measurements is possible. It is an important point to be able to obtain more reli-
able energy difference estimates. It might be done by including artifact look-a-like
components from SVD analysis in a dedicated model fit. Another artifact reduction
might be accomplished by using harsher outlier detection criteria. However, due
to the few measurements at each time delay, it is limited how much data can be
removed for some of the systems.
• Use the Neighbouring Similarity Method on other metal complexes to test and verify
the observed tendency of ”good predicted Q-ranges”. This is in order to investigate
the influence of choosing different Q-ranges as self normalisation intervals.
• Investigate whether a potential correlation between the difference scattering signal
from the solvent-only and density change response exists. This information might
tell how these contributions should be included in the model.
• Investigate the influence on the measurements when varying the X-ray spot positions
inside the optical laser spot. Basically solve the question: how to get robust against
a potential alignment problem.

APPENDIX A
Computational details appendix
Appendix A.1 provides an analysis of the observed offset on the simulated RDFs. Ap-
pendix A.2 investigates the influence of different damping parameters.
Appendix A.3
shows the simulated difference scattering signal for the cross-term and solvent-only term
with the chosen damping parameters for the different systems.
A.1
Analysis of the RDFs offset
In this section, the RDFs are investigated further to check whether the requirement from
Equation (3.2) is fulfilled.
Figure A.1 illustrates a zoom of Figure 3.2 around g(r) = 1.
The black dashed line
show the average g(r) value in the interval r = [18; 25] Å. There is an offset between the
black dashed line and g(r) = 1. This indicates that the requirement from Equation (3.2)
is not fulfilled for the RDF between the Fe-atom and solvent O-atom. Investigating all
(solute,solvent) RDFs, offsets are observed.
Figure A.2 illustrates a zoom around g(r) = 1 for RDF between two solvent atoms. Once
more an offset can be observed. Comparing the offset from the (solute,solvent) RDFs to
the (solvent,solvent) RDFs, the offset are larger for the (solute,solvent) RDFs.
Figure A.3 shows an example after the RDFs have been renormalised when using Equa-
tion (3.4). As it can be seen the offset is removed, making the requirement in Equation (3.2)
fulfilled. All the RDFs used to calculate the scattering signal have therefore undergone a
renormalisation based on Equation (3.4).
Figure A.4 illustrates the difference scattering signal simulated on RDFs with a renormal-
isation (blue curve) and without a renormalisation (red curve). It can be observed that
the two curves defers at low Q-values (Q < 0.3 Å−1).
Figure A.1: Zoom of the RDFs shown in Figure 3.2. The RDF is between the solute
Fe-atom and the solvent O-atom. The black dashed line marks the average of the g(r) in
the r-range [18;25] Å. Please notice that the average g(r) is different from one showing an
offset.

A.2.
INVESTIGATION OF DIFFERENT DAMPING PARAMETERS
A.2
Investigation of different damping parameters
In this section the influence of the damping parameters are examined.
Figure A.5 illustrated the influence on changing the rcut. The right hand side of Figure A.5
can be used to see which rcut value seems reasonable. rcut = 15 Å is too large since clear
artifacts can be seen by straight lines at increasing r. This is also seen in the blue curve
where no damping is performed. At large r the curve blows up which will give artifacts
at small Q when calculated to scattering signal.
rcut = 7 and rcut = 10 seems all right.

Figure A.5:
Visualising the influence of different use of rcut in damping the (renor-

low spin geometry simulation between two solvent oxygen atoms subtracted with 1. Cen-
ter: A zoom of the left side figure. The influence of different damping values can be seen.
Right side: The in side of the integral in Equation (3.8).
Figure A.6 and Figure A.7 shows the investigation of varying the rmax and rcut value
in the calculated difference scattering signal.
The biggest influence when varying rcut
and rmax can be seen in the simulated solvent-only difference scattering. Having a too
large rcut introduces more likely artificial oscillations due to numerical noise becomes more
dominating at large r values. Having a too small rcut, smoothen the curve, risking that
structural information is lost in the damping.

APPENDIX B
Experimental conditions and Data overview
In this chapter, a clarification for the estimated flow rate and experimental resolution
mentioned in Chapter 4 is given.
Furthermore, waterfall plots for six other systems,
investigated than the one shown in Section 4.3, can be seen.
B.1
Flow rate
In the logbook it is written: ”Sample flow is 45 ml in 16 minutes 15 seconds at a pump
speed of 10 with 200 um capillary”.
If it is assumed that the sample flow can be modelled as a perfect cylinder with a diameter
D = 200 µm and a volume V = 45 mL water, then the height of the cylinder can be
calculated as:
height =
V
π

D
2
2 ≈1400 m
(B.1)
The time for the volume to flow is t = (16·60+15) s. The flow rate can then be estimated
to:
v = height
t
≈1.5m
s
(B.2)
This is off course an estimation since, when the sample leaves the nozzle, the liquid jet
will be free-standing, and the shape can therefore change a bit. It might get a thinner
diameter, which will make the height of the cylinder longer and thereby the flow rate
higher than the estimated in Equation (B.2). The flow rate is usually set around a flow
-rate of 3 m/s and is tuned to get the most stable liquid sheet [20].
B.2
Experimental Time Resolution
The time resolution depends on the duration of the laser pulse ∆tlaser, the duration of the
X-ray pulses ∆tX−ray and the thickness of the sample ∆tjet.
∆tlaser and ∆tX−ray arer given in Table 4.1. ∆tjet is caused by the velocity mismatch
between the optical laser pulse and the X-ray pulse propagate through the sample. The
velocity in the sample is dependent on the refractive index, n, which depends on the energy
of propagating electromagnetic wave and the sample, v = c/n and c is the speed of light.
The refractive index for the optical pulse in water is nwater, 560 nm = 1.331 [65]. For X-rays
the refractive index is n0.7 Å ≈1 [66]. ∆tX−ray can then be calculated by:
∆tjet = lsample
c
 n560 nm −n0.7 Å
 = 300 · 10−6m
3 · 108 m
s
· 0.3 ≈0.3 ps
(B.3)
where lsample is the thickness of the sample.
∆t =
q
(∆tlaser)2 + (∆tX−ray)2 + (∆tjet)2 ≈100 ps
(B.4)
The time resolution is approximated to 100 ps being mostly limited by the duration of the
X-ray pulse. Since the smallest time step in the experiment is 200 ps, the time resolution
of 100 ps is a reasonable resolution.

APPENDIX D
Self Normalisation
Figures D.1 to D.7 show the Neighbouring Similarity Method (described in Section 5.4.1)
performed on all seven derivatives of [Fe(terpy)2]2+ for a time delay of 200 ps. The different
colors mark different number of used intervals. As described in Section 5.4.1, a good self
normalisation interval is expected to be seen in the regions of low RMSE.
In Figure D.2 the method has a diﬀicult time of finding a good area. This suggest the
data to be very noise.
In general, self normalisation interval of 1 Å seems to be reasonable.

APPENDIX E
Q-scaling
In this section the reduced χ2, as function of the correction percentages, is shown for all
other systems at a time delay of −3 µs than illustrated in Section 6.3.2 in Figures E.2, E.4,
E.6, E.8, E.10 and E.12. Furthermore, fits and residual without and with Q-correction are
shown for the seven systems in Figures E.1, E.3, E.5, E.7, E.9, E.11 and E.13. The impor-
tance of the Q-correction is apparent in the residuals from the fits without Q-correction.
There is no big difference between the Q-correction optimized for the measurement at the
time delay -3 µs and the Q-correction determined as the median of the 5 latest times. This
shows some robustness of the chosen median Q-corrected value.

marks a 4th order polynomial used to find the correction percentage. The minimum of the
polynomial indicates the best yielded fit.

System=FeT-COOH, Scaling factor SF=11.6  106
Calculated Scalc, GS(Q)
SF Measured Smeas(Q, t=-3 s)
Figure F.1: Illustration of the scaling to one liquid unit cell (LUC). The left hand side
shows the different scattering contribution included in the total calculated scattering signal
for one LUC. The right hand side shows the total calculated scattering signal (red graph)
together the median of the measured absolute scattering signal at a delay time ∆t = −3 µs
times a scaling factor (purple graph). The scaling factor is determined by fitting the blue
graph to the red in the Q-interval =[1.7;8] Å−1. The best fit provides a scaling factor of
11,648,027, which is used to transform the unit of the measured data into electron units
per liquid unit cell.

APPENDIX G
Model Fit
In this chapter, all the fits for the other systems that have not been shown in Chapter 7 are
presented for Model 2C, Model 3B and Model 4 in Appendices G.1 to G.3, respectively. In
Appendix G.4 the corresponding kinetic fits are plotted. The kinetic fits are fitted on the
excitation fraction values and temperature values extracted from the Model 2C, Model 3B
and Model 4.
G.1
Model 2C
In this section all fits in relation to Model 2C are presented. Except for system FeT-H,
the individual fits are illustrated for all the systems in Figures G.1 to G.6.
